M.Sc. Thesis
Master of Science in Sustainable Energy
Energy communities: scenario analyses for
optimal and fair PV-battery operation
Paula Riera Turro (s192498)
Kongens Lyngby 2022
==PAGE:2==
DTU Compute
Department of Applied Mathematics and Computer Science Dynamical Systems
Technical University of Denmark
Produktionstorvet
Building 303B
2800 Kongens Lyngby, Denmark
==PAGE:3==
Summary
Energy communities (ECs) are a tool to democratize the energy transition and provide
consumers with an active role regarding their energy use. However, few studies have pro-
vided a comprehensive overview of collective batteries associated with PV. This Master
Thesis uses social acceptance theory to analyse the political, market, and community
acceptance of energy communities of shared self-consumption with PV generation and
collective batteries in Catalonia, Spain.
Political and market acceptance are studied through the development of a MILP that
minimizes the investment and operational costs of an EC. The model is developed to
analyze the benefits of collective batteries, understand how different legislation affects
the profitability of such assets, evaluate how uncertainty in electricity prices and battery
costs affect the investment decision, and investigate different rules to share the energy
between members. Community acceptance is assessed through the theoretical frame-
work of energy justice by conducting interviews, participant observation, and analyzing
documentation of existing ECs in Catalonia.
The results show that shared self-consumption with collective batteries has political,
market and community acceptance.
Investing in PV and batteries together is more
profitable than investing in PV capacity. Adding a battery increases self-consumption,
self-suﬀiciency and savings by 10%.
However, the profitability of batteries depends
highly on the investment costs, with costs beyond 522 €/kWh yielding negative NPV.
Additionally, if legislation allowed arbitrage, the business model would become more
attractive, increasing the NPV by 5,000 € and doubling the installed battery capacity.
On the one hand, sending the same amount of energy to all the members results in
a fairer distribution of savings among members, with most of the members receiving
between 200 and 300 €/year. However, this sharing rule sends between 8,000 and 13,000
kWh/year more energy to the grid. Consequently, it reduces the profitability of investing
in a battery. On the other hand, sending energy to members based on their hourly
demand increases self-consumption, self-suﬀiciency and savings for the EC. However,
savings are unevenly distributed between members.
Even though there is community acceptance, the conceptual definition of energy
community is still being contested and concerns regarding the municipality being the
primary beneficiary of the project were voiced. Trust in the developer is critical for
the project’s success.
However, it also results in stakeholders being less involved in
the project’s development. Finally, the addition of batteries to ECs might change the
perceptions of fairness with a particular focus on stakeholders’ environmental concerns
regarding batteries.

==PAGE:12==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
The transformation of the energy system is one of the key factors to achieve climate
neutrality before 2050. The Clean Energy Package (CEP) is European Union’s (EU)
policy framework that paves the way for a gradual transition toward a carbon-neutral
economy. Specifically, it updates the EU targets for 2030 to a 55% cut in greenhouse
gas (GHG) compared to 1990, 32% of renewable energy sources in the energy mix [1]
and 32.5% of energy eﬀiciency target compared to 2007 [2].
The EU requires that each member state elaborates a 10 year National energy and cli-
mate plan from 2021 to 2030. The plan outlines how the EU countries intend to address
energy eﬀiciency, renewables, greenhouse gas emission reductions, interconnections, and
research and innovation. The integrated national energy and climate plan (PNIEC for
its acronym in Spanish) in Spain expects to achieve 42% of renewable penetration re-
garding final energy use and a 74% of renewable generation in the electric sector by 2030.
Additionally, EU member states are required to develop a long-term national strategy.
Based on the PNIEC, the Long Term Decarbonization Strategy (ELP for its Spanish
acronym) describes the roadmap to transform the energy system from 2030 to 2050 to
achieve climate neutrality before 2050. The Department of Climate Action, Food and
Rural Agenda of the Catalan Government has estimated that the minimum contribution
of Catalonia to the GHG emissions reduction should be 40% [3]. However, in Catalonia,
generation from renewable energies in 2020 remains limited to 8% [4].
One mechanism to achieve the climate targets defined by the CEP are energy commu-
nities (EC). Additionally, the CEP introduced energy communities as a tool to empower
citizens to be part of the energy transition. One type of energy communities are shared
self-consumption initiatives. Shared-self consumption consists of one or more renewable
generation installations and a group of consumers in the vicinity of the installation
who share its production. Self-consumption can contribute to better integration and
social acceptance of energy infrastructure in Spain, reduce the losses in transportation
and distribution, utilize unused urban spaces and create climate and energy awareness
among citizens [5]. Additionally, it was estimated that half of the EU citizens could
self-consume - individual and shared - by 2050 and cover 45% of the electric demand [6].
However, an electricity grid with a high penetration of renewable technologies re-
quires flexibility services due to their stochastic nature. Consequently, flexibility be-
comes crucial in the electricity sector of the near future. The Regulation (EU) 2019/943
about the internal EU electricity market and the Directive (EU) 2019/944 about com-
mon rules for the internal electricity market, establish a new market configuration that
will promote flexibility services. Additionally, the Directive (EU) 2019/944 introduces
the figure of the aggregator. The aggregator is ”a natural or legal person who combines
==PAGE:13==
1.1 Problem statement and project scope
2
multiple customer loads or generated electricity for sale, purchase or auction in any
electricity market”. Thus, the aggregator allows energy communities and small produc-
ers to access electricity markets. Consequently, active consumers, demand response and
storage become vital elements in the new regulatory framework. Moreover, the PNIEC
envisions the development of storage as one of the key tools to provide flexibility, con-
tribute to the management of the electric grid and enable the citizens to participate in
the energy transition.
Consequently, energy communities together with storage, such as collective batteries,
have the potential to become an important tool to democratize the energy sector, bring
flexibility to the electricity system, provide balancing services to the grid through an
aggregator, increase savings for consumers and contribute to achieving climate targets.
1.1
Problem statement and project scope
Energy communities with collective batteries are a new and innovative initiative that is
starting to be implemented in Spain. The purpose of this Thesis is to conduct a holistic
analysis of the social acceptance of an energy community with a collective battery in Cat-
alonia, Spain. Social acceptance has three dimensions: political, market and community
acceptance. Na Li et al. [7] define the three components of social acceptance as follows.
Political acceptance refers to the acceptance of technologies, policies, and institutional
changes by key stakeholders and policy actors. Market acceptance reflects the accep-
tance of various stakeholders (consumers, investors, and other relevant stakeholders) in
the commercialization process of the project. Finally, community acceptance addresses
the issue to what extent the decisions are acceptable to local stakeholders, residents, and
local authorities of the local-based projects.
Political and market acceptance are analyzed through regulation and documentation
review and by developing an optimization model for an EC with a collective battery.
The model considers the minimization of investment and operational costs of an EC with
solar production and battery for self-consumption. This means that participation of the
battery in electricity markets is not included, given that Spanish legislation regarding
batteries’ access to the markets is still not developed.
Additionally, the model has
multiple purposes:
• Analyze the benefits from an energy and economic perspective of collective batter-
ies.
• Understand how different legislation affects the profitability of such investments.
• Evaluate how uncertainty in electricity prices and battery costs affect the invest-
ment decision.
• Investigate different rules to share the energy between members to identify winners
and losers.
==PAGE:14==
1.1 Problem statement and project scope
3
Community acceptance is assessed through the theoretical framework of energy justice.
The perception of justice is evaluated by conducting interviews, participant observation
and analyzing documentation of existing ECs in Catalonia. Neither of the ECs investi-
gated had implemented batteries. Therefore, energy justice is explored for existing EC
without batteries, while the discussion delves into how adding a collective battery to an
EC will affect the perceptions of fairness by the main stakeholders.
The contents of this Thesis are briefly summarized below.
Chapter 2 provides a summary of the concepts and background information used
throughout the Thesis. This includes the definition of energy communities by the EU
Directives and Spanish legislation, the benefits and purpose of adding storage to the
electricity system, relevant legislation regarding batteries in Spain, and the theoretical
framework regarding modeling with uncertainty.
Chapter 3 introduces the State of the Art within academia regarding energy com-
munities, storage and energy justice.
The section reviews the economics of EC, the
operation of storage with a focus on collective storage, the dilemma of sharing energy
within the EC, uncertainty within energy communities and the theoretical framework of
social acceptance and energy justice.
Chapter 4 describe the three optimization models developed, the methodology used
for the feasibility study and the methods used to gather data and analyse it for the
community acceptance study.
Chapter 5 defines the case description - including the type of energy commu-
nity considered for the analysis, the data inputs for the optimization model their pre-
processing - and the case studies used to assess the effects of prices, legislation and
sharing rules.
Chapter 6 provides the results obtained from the optimization model for the dif-
ferent case studies described in chapter 5 and the results regarding the perception of
justice by key stakeholders.
Chapter 7 delves deeper into the interpretation of the results in the context of
social acceptance of EC with collective batteries. This includes how EC with collective
batteries are expected to develop in the future years considering legislation, political
context, price developments, and how collective batteries in EC might be perceived by
local stakeholders.
Chapter 8 summarizes the main findings and conclusions to be extracted from this
research.
==PAGE:15==
CHAPTER 2
Background
This chapter introduces the concepts and theory that are used throughout the document.
This includes: the definition of EC in the European context and in Spain, together with
the status quo of EC in Spain; the role of storage and batteries in the future energy
sector as well as the regulatory framework in Spain; and the modelling technique used
to include uncertainty.
2.1
Energy Communities
With the Clean Energy package for all (CEP), the European Union is changing the
role of consumers from passive to active. One of the tools to achieve that objective are
Energy Communities (ECs). The CEP defines ECs based on three main characteristics
[8]:
• Governance: ECs are legal entities that are open to all potential local members
based on non-discriminatory criteria and voluntary participation.
• Ownership and control: effective control by citizens, local authorities and
smaller business whose primary economic activity is not the energy sector.
• Purpose: primary purpose is to provide environmental, economic or social bene-
fits rather than focus on financial profits.
Additionally, community members are allowed to leave the community under fair
conditions, meaning that Member States should find the right balance between the right
to leave of members and the solubility of the EC.
The Member States must ensure to implement a favourable framework for the imple-
mentation of ECs. This includes the reduction of unjustified legislative and administra-
tive barriers, ensuring that EC are not discriminated against carrying out their activities,
fair authorization and registry procedures, fair and transparent grid tariffs and access to
financing and information [6]. Finally, an EC should be able to access a market directly
without being discriminated, as a self-representative entity, or through a third party
energy service provider such as an aggregator [9].
Two types of ECs are defined, the Directive (EU) 2018/2001 on the promotion of the
use of energy from renewable sources introduces Renewable Energy Communities (REC)
and the Directive (EU) 2019/944 on common rules for the internal market for electricity
==PAGE:16==
2.1 Energy Communities
5
defines Citizen Energy Communities (CEC), based on the following criteria. Figure 2.1
summarizes the main differences between both.
• Geographical scope: while CECs are not tied to a geographical scope, RECs
are organized in the proximity of renewable energy projects that are owned and
developed by the community. Therefore, for RECs the controlling members need
to be within the ”proximity” of the renewable energy projects of the community.
However, the definition of the geographical scope should be suﬀiciently open to
allow for local conditions to be taken into account, such as rural with respect to
urban areas.
• Activities: CECs operate within the electricity sector and can be renewable or
fossil-fuel based, while RECs can cover both electricity and heating sectors. Addi-
tionally, Figure 2.1 shows the activities that CECs and RECS can perform.
• Participants: RECs have a more restricted membership compared to CECs. Only
natural persons, local authorities and micro, small and medium-sized entreprises
whose participation is not their primary economic activity are allowed. On the
other hand, CECs allow all actors to participate, as long as, large-scale enterprises
whose primary economic activity is within the energy sector do not have decision-
making power.
• Autonomy: RECs must be autonomus regarding any member’s private interest.
Therefore, the internal decision-making should be democratic and ensure that all
members are adequately represented. The definition of CECs does not include
autonomy, however large energy companies should not have decision-making power.
Only RECs have been transposed to the Spanish legislation. However, the guide for
the development of ECs published by the Institute for Diversification and Energy Sav-
ings (IDAE for its acronym in Spanish) does not differentiate between CECs and REcs.
Given the legislative uncertainty and following the convention of IDAE, throughout this
document the term energy community will be used.
ECs can have multiple benefits for different actors such as members, TSO and DSO
or the territories where this entities are implemented. For instance, ECs not only con-
tribute achieving the the energy targets of the EU, but also democratize the energy
transition by bringing the energy sector closer to the citizens and allowing them to
participate and decide. Additionally, ECs have the potential to bring multiple social
benefits to the community, such as, increasing the knowledge about the energy sector,
bringing local value by helping implement local sustainable projects, generating financial
returns to the community and social cohesion [9, 11, 12]. Additionally, ECs benefit from
economies of scale in the cases with collective generation and storage, gaining greater
bargaining power (e.g. bulk buying of commodities) or taking the opportunity to gain
revenues from markets for which they would otherwise not qualify (e.g. from balanc-
ing markets through aggregation) [9]. Morover, ECs when associated with storage, can
supply multiple services to the grid such as peak shaving and balancing services.
==PAGE:17==
2.1 Energy Communities
6
Figure 2.1: Differences between RECs and CECs [10].
Despite regulation about EC being in its infancy in most European countries, many
initiatives have been developing in the past years, mostly through a bottom-up approach.
In Europe, there were about 3500 renewable energy cooperatives in 2019 [8]. These
energy communities present a wide variety of set-ups: shared self consumption; energy
cooperatives; community flexibility aggregation where, for example, consumer’s batteries
are aggregated to participate in balancing markets; local peer-to-peer markets or e-
mobility cooperatives [13].
The European Directives have to be transposed to national legislation by every Mem-
ber State. In Spain, renewable energy communities in the Royal-Decree (RD) 23/2020
are defined as:
Renewable energy communities are legal entities based on the open and vol-
untary participation, autonomous and effectively controlled by members or
members that are in the proximity of the renewable energy projects and that
are owners of said legal entities, that the members are natural persons, SMES
==PAGE:18==
2.1 Energy Communities
7
or local authorities, included municipalities, and that the main activity is to
provide environmental, economic, social benefits to its members or in the
locality where they operate, instead of financial gains.
However, the legislation regarding ECs rights and obligations, as well as their role
as a legal entity has not been developed yet. Despite the legal uncertainty, initiatives
have been flourishing through the territory. For instance, SomEnergia, an energy coop-
erative, with its initiative Generació kWh allows the members to invest in a part of a
renewable asset. Then, the proportional part of the energy produced is sold at the cost
of generation and compensated annually on their electricity bill. Each project is owned
by Som Energia’s limited company, and its production is sold via a bilateral contract to
the cooperative, which in turn redistributes the kWh to each participant [8]. Moreover,
there is a willingness by the government to promote EC. The Ministry for the Ecological
Transition and the Demographic Challenge (Ministerio para la Transición Ecológica y
el Reto Demográfico), through IDAE, has designed an ecosystem of support for energy
communities, with a fund of 100 million euros from the Recuperation, Transformation
and Resilience Plan. The Plan of Recuperation, Transformation and Resilience (PRTR)
is the Spanish strategy to canalize the EU next Generation funds to repair the damages
caused by the Covid-19 pandemic. The Plan includes the energy transition as one of the
ten policies to promote in a post-Covid context. The IDAE, as the entity in charge of
managing the part of the Next Generation funds, has developed an interactive map to
detect the initiatives that are being developed within the Spanish territory [14].
Furthermore, the RD 244/2019 on self-consumption was modified to include self-
consumption to a group of people beyond single owners. Thus, a self-consumption facil-
ity’s production may be shared with nearby consumers [8]. Given that legislation about
ECs is still under development, the changes in self-consumption legislation have been
the base to develop energy community initiatives for collective generation of renewable
energy. This legislation allows consumers to connect administratively to the same PV
installation without paying grid fees or tolls, and therefore, allowing all the consumers
connected to it to realize important savings on their bills. One condition is that the leg-
islation sets a maximum radius to join the collective installation of 500m. Additionally,
the excess energy that is not self-consumed can be exported to the grid. Consumers have
multiple choices regarding evacuating the excess energy and economic compensation:
• Not evacuating the energy to the grid. In this case, the installation needs to have
a system to avoid evacuating energy to the grid.
• Evacuating the energy to the grid, in this case the consumer can decide between
different economic compensation schemes. For energy communities the whole com-
munity has to agree to one compensation scheme.
– Without compensation: the energy sent to the grid is not paid for.
– With compensation
∗Simplified compensation (compensación simplificada in Spanish): the
member’s retailer pays the excess energy at a fixed price.
The self-
==PAGE:19==
2.2 Storage
8
consumed energy and revenues from selling excess energy are discounted
from the consumer’s bill. Under no circumstance the bill of the consumer
can be below zero. This means that once the member bill reaches zero,
the extra excess energy is sold at price 0.
∗Without simplified compensation: In this case the energy community sells
the excess energy at the market price. However, the EC needs to register
as an energy producer and report the revenues in their tax statement.
Additionally, the RD 244/2019 about self-consumption determines how energy is to
be shared within the EC. Until recently, it only allowed fixed sharing coeﬀicients. This
means, that for each hour of the day the energy from the solar panels needs to be
shared based on those coeﬀicients. For example, in an EC with 2 members it is agreed
that member 1 receives 60% and member 2 receives 40% of the PV production. As
a consequence, even if consumer 1 is not at home and can not consume the energy, if
there is solar production, member 1 will receive 60% of that production. When that
occurs, member 1 sells their1 energy to the grid.
Recently, dynamic coeﬀicients ex-
ante have been introduced in legislation. This means, that for each hour of the year,
the members agree on how the energy will be shared before the energy is delivered.
However, the new legislation is not enforced and consumers can still decide to use fixed
sharing coeﬀicients. Finally, legislation is being developed to allow dynamic sharing
coeﬀicients ex-post, meaning that the energy produced will be shared based on real time
consumption.
2.2
Storage
In a distributed energy sector with high penetration of renewable generation, storage will
play a central role to ensure security of supply, grid safety and an effective integration of
RES [5]. The stochastic and variable nature of renewable energy makes flexibility tools
necessary for the system, one of them is energy storage .
Energy storage can provide services to different actors of the energy sector. [15] give
an overview of these potential services. As mentioned above, energy storage is crucial
to ensuring stability to the grid, thus, providing services to the TSO and DSO. These
services include: frequency balancing, spin/no-spin reserves, voltage support, load follow-
ing, black start and capacity reserves. Additionally, transmission grid upgrade deferral
which consist on using energy storage to solve congestion issues instead of upgrading the
transmission grid. Moreover, storage might have added benefits for future local markets
by being placed in strategic locations where there is often line congestion. Furthermore,
storage performing arbitrage - charging when the prices are low and discharging when
high - might provide wholesale electricity markets with more stable and less volatile
prices. Storage allows combined heat and power (CHP) plants to uncouple their heat and
electricity, allowing them to be more flexible and have more strategic behaviours when
1They is used as the gender neutral pronoun.
==PAGE:20==
2.2 Storage
9
participating in markets. Additionally, storage when coupled with conventional power
plants provides support with optimizing the operation providing generation bridging -
pick up generation load when a generator is stopping until another starts, or avoiding
stopping a unit - or generator ramping - pick up strong and fast load variations giving
time for a given generator to ramp up. For renewable technologies, storage allows the
minimization of curtailment and provides the possibility of doing arbitrage.
At a consumer level the main services are: self-consumption maximization when
paired with PV; peak-shaving, thus using the battery when there are peaks of consump-
tion to reduce the electricity bill; time-of-use energy cost management by reducing the
electricity bill by charging from the grid when the prices are low and discharging when
high; and continuity of energy supply. In the context of energy communities, storage
can maximize self-consumption when paired with PV production; improve the fairness
of distribution of benefits between members by providing energy for members who do
not consume when there is PV production; optimization of peer-to-peer (P2P) markets
by storing community energy independently to its ownership to be used later, while set-
tling up the costs and revenues ex-post; islanding operation by using the storage during
outages; and access to extra revenues by participating in balancing markets through an
aggregator.
Within storage, batteries are one of the technologies that are most flexible and most
easily adaptable to the needs of different actors. This is because they are sizable, mean-
ing that their capacity can range from kWh to MWh. The 13 services that batteries
can provide to different stakeholders are summarized in Figure 2.2, demonstrating how
batteries are one of the most adaptable energy storage technologies. Moreover, declin-
ing battery costs and increasing performance has resulted in massive investments in
the advancement of battery technology ecosystems which is pushing batteries for new
applications more quickly than anticipated [16]. Among the different types of battery
technologies, li-ion batteries are expected to dominate the market in the following years
due to their rapid performance improvements [17] coupled with dramatic cost reductions
[16].
In the Spanish context, the PNIEC expects the development of storage as a key
tool to provide flexibility to the electric system by supporting an increase of renewable
capacity, contributing to managing the electricity grid and promoting the participation
of citizen in the energy transition. The PNIEC and ELP determine that the storage
capacity in Spain should increase from 8.3 GW - mostly provided by pumped hydro -
to a minimum of 20 GW in 2030 and 30 GW in 2050. These targets include large-scale
daily and weekly storage, storage behind the meter and seasonal storage. For behind-
the-meter storage, the objective is 400 MW for 2030.
With the objective of developing the vision of PNIEC, a National Storage Strategy
was approved on the 9th of February of 2021. The Strategy conducts a technical analysis
of storage, identifies the current challenges for energy storage, the action plan to achieve
the objectives and the opportunities storage represents for the energy system. During
the elaboration of the Strategy, some first steps were taken towards transforming the
regulatory framework in line with EU Directives. However, the Storage Strategy con-
cludes that it still is necessary to adapt the regulation to the new implicit characteristics
==PAGE:21==
2.2 Storage
10
Figure 2.2: Services provided by batteries [18].
of storage technologies. Additionally, the Strategy finds that one key challenge will be
changes in regulation regarding the access of storage to current and future electricity
markets. In conclusion, specific regulation for storage in Spain to ensure that there are
no extra barriers for such technologies is sill to be developed. More information about
the regulation on storage in Spain can be found in Appendix A.
==PAGE:22==
2.3 Stochastic programming
11
Given that the PRTR identifies three main needs - to promote projects of electric
infrastructure, smart grids and deployment of flexibility - an increase of investment in
storage and flexibility tools is expected in the next years due to a context of public
funding in infrastructure.
In the context of storage behind-the-meter, the Self-consumption Road Map [19]
expects that storage paired with PV production will increase the economic potential of
self-consumption by 2.39 GW in 2030 which represents a 16% increase compared to a case
without storage. Regarding the addition of storage in self-consumption initiatives, the
RD 244/2019 on self-consumption, establishes that storage can be installed associated
to the generation, one of the consumers or directly to the grid. However, storage is only
allowed to contribute to the distribution between members of the shared energy unless
its associated to the generation.
2.3
Stochastic programming
Uncertainty is inherent when considering the energy sector, which affects the energy
production of renewable technologies, consumer electricity demand or electricity prices.
In this Thesis stochastic programming is the modelling technique used to include electri-
city price uncertainty. Stochastic programming is a mathematical modeling framework
both to characterize the uncertainty and to derive informed decisions [20].
2.3.1
Two-stage stochastic programming
Two stage stochastic programs are divided in two stages. The first-stage variables x
are decided before the realization of uncertainty, thus they are the same for all cases
of uncertainty.
On the other hand, the second-stage variables are decided after the
realization of the uncertainty is known. Therefore, in the first-stage one has to solve for
the expected value across scenarios (ω) [20]:
Min
cTx + E[Q(ω)]
(2.1)

Ax = b
(2.2)
x ∈X
(2.3)
Where x are the first-stage variables with a solution space of X , A is the matrix of
constraint coeﬀicients, b the right-hand side vector, c the vector of objective coeﬀicients
and Q is the recourse function:
Q(ω) =
min{q(ω)Ty(ω)|T(ω)x + W(ω)y(ω) = h(ω), y(ω) ∈Y }, ∀ω ∈Ω
(2.4)

objective coeﬀicients of the second-stage variables, T(ω) the constraint coeﬀicients of
the first-stage decision variable, the constraint coeﬀicients of the second-stage decision
variable W(ω) and h(ω) is the right hand side vector. The deterministic equivalent
problem is written as:

where π(ω) is the probability of scenario ω. However, this formulation requires that
the space of possible scenarios of uncertainty is discretisised into a finite number of
scenarios. The methodology used to do so in this Thesis is described below.
2.3.2
Scenario generation
As mentioned above, evaluating a solution on all possible outcomes of uncertainty is
frequently impossible. For this reason, discretising the distribution of a random variable
using a finite set of possible outcomes is indispensable. Creating an appropriate repre-
sentation of a continuous random variable using a finite set of values can be challenging.
In this study bootstrapping is used as a technique to generate scenarios. Bootstrap-
ping is a technique first developed by [21] for inferential purposes. However it was [22]
who proposed bootstrapping as a technique for scenario generation. The method com-
bines the use of historical data with bootstrapping technique. It consists on sampling
random observations from the historical data until the desired length of the scenario
is reached. This can be done as many times as needed to create as many scenarios in
order to have an appropriate representation of the historical data. Another variation,
the block bootstrapping technique, allows to retain original data correlations between
periods. In this case, instead of an individual observation, a block is sampled. The idea
is to chose a block length that captures the observed correlation between the historic
data. Blocks are sampled until the desired length of the scenario is reached.
Algorithm 1 Bootstrapping
1: ω ←1
2: while ω ≤Ωdo
3:
t ←1
4:
while t ≤T do
5:
Draw random block from historical data
6:
St,w = add random block
7:
t ←t + b
8:
ω ←ω + 1
==PAGE:24==
2.3 Stochastic programming
13
Where ω is the scenario created with the bootstrapping technique, b is the length of
the block T is the length of the scenario.
2.3.3
Scenario reduction
As mentioned above, a large number of scenarios is required to get a representative
sample with similar properties as the original distribution. However, a large number
of scenarios increases the complexity of the stochastic program drastically. As a conse-
quence scenario reduction techniques are necessary.
In this study, the Partitioning Around Medoids (PAM) or k-medoids clustering is
used to reduce the number of scenarios generated with the bootstrapping technique,
using the implementation of [23]. PAM is a clustering technique introduced by [24].
Clustering methods are unsupervised machine learning techniques used to reduce data
points to representative ones. [24] write the problem as follows
Min

i=1

PAM finds the K data points from the original data set so that the objects in each
cluster show a high degree of similarity. d(i, j) is the distance or dissimilarity between
points i and j.
yi shows if a point is chosen as a medoid and zi,j which points are
assigned to which medoids. The problem is solved in two parts: in the first stage, K
points are initialized as medoids; in the second part, the data points are assigned to
the closest medoid, after in each cluster a new medoid is selected that minimizes the
distance between them. The process keeps going until no new medoids are selected.

2.3.4
Solution evaluation
Stochastic programming increases the complexity of a problem, thus it is important to
justify why it is needed instead of a deterministic approach. Widely used metrics to
do so are the Expected Value of Perfect Information (EVPI), Value of the Stochastic
Solution (VSS) [25] and Out-of-sample test [20].
Expected value of perfect information (EVPI)
It represents the maximum amount a decision maker would be ready to pay in return
for complete and accurate information about the future [25]. It is calculated as follows
given that RP is the objective value of the stochastic problem and WS is the expected
value wait-and-see approach, where perfect information is available and the scenarios
are solved individually.
EV PI = RP −WS
(2.15)
Value of stochastic solution (VSS)
Shows the benefit of using stochastic programming compared to using expected values. It
is calculated as follows for a minimization problem, where the RP is the objective value
of the stochastic problem. The EEV is obtained by first determining the first-stage
variables using the expected values of the uncertain parameters.
Then, the original
stochastic problems can be solved fixing first-stage variables with the values obtained
with the deterministic. The optimal value of the modified stochastic problem is the
EEV.
V SS = EEV −RP
(2.16)
Out-of-sample test
The objective is to check the robustness, in other words, the applicability of the solution
in new scenarios. To do so, the solution is evaluated for scenarios that are possible but
not considered in the optimization. Thus, the stochastic problem is solved to obtain
the first-variable solution. Then the first-stage variables are fixed and the solution is
evaluated for a number of new scenarios of the uncertain parameter.
==PAGE:26==
CHAPTER 3
State of the Art
The Green Energy Package enables energy communities to become a vehicle towards a
carbon neutral and more democratic energy sector. Energy communities are expected to
provide several benefits to multiple actors: from bringing the energy transition closer to
the citizen, to helping balance the grid. Additionally, EC have the capability of reducing
energy costs and increasing autonomy for the members [26].
This section reviews the latest research regarding the economics of EC, operation
of storage with a focus on collective storage, the dilemma of sharing energy within
the EC, uncertainty within energy communities and the theoretical framework of social
acceptance and energy justice.
3.1
Economics of EC
The term energy community is used as unique concept, however, it includes a wide
variety of set-ups. For instance, [13] defines eight business model archetypes: energy
cooperatives, community prosumerism, local energy markets, community collective gen-
eration, third-party-sponsored communities, community flexibility aggregation, commu-
nity ESCO and E-mobility cooperatives. As a consequence of such diversity together
with the different local electricity tariffs, legislation and the size of the system [9], the
feasibility of energy communities varies significantly within the literature.
The economic impacts of EC has been assessed through a wide variety of indicators:
• Levelized cost of energy [27, 28, 29]
• Payback period [28, 30, 31]
• Internal rate of return (IRR) [32, 33, 34]
• Life cycle cost [35]
• Net Present Value (NPV) [36, 37]
• Total cost savings [38, 39, 40, 41, 42, 43, 44]
• Investment cost savings [45]
• Operation costs savings [46]
• Energy bill savings [47, 48, 37, 49, 50, 51, 52, 53, 54]
==PAGE:27==
3.2 Storage
16
Energy bill savings is an intuitive indicator to describe the economic performance
of EC and can be directly understood by its members.
Multi-apartment residential
buildings with PVs or PVs with batteries can result in bill savings of 90 €/year in
Austria, 970 €/year in Germany [49] and 437 €/year in Finland [51]. Moreover, 50%
more PV capacity is installed when the members are part of an EC, thus increasing the
self-consumption [51]. PVs with batteries in rural single family houses result in a 5%
cost reduction [49], while households with the same technology in Masachussets have
increased savings by 34 $/year [52]. Total system costs are also found to be reduced by
24% in [39]. Similar results are found in [44] with a cost reduction of 25% and in [42]
with a cost reduction of 32%. Operation costs savings are found in [46] to be reduced
between 0.08% and 16% in buildings and a cost decline of 30% in district cooling [38].
However, environmental objectives might reduce the cost reduction [40], therefore lower
environmental impacts result in higher costs.
Other works have reported the economic performance of EC based on typical in-
dicators used in project planing, such as, NPV, IRR, payback period or LCOE. By
maximizing the NPV, [37] find that for PV systems, the profitability increases when
forming EC compared to individual buildings. The payback period of complex systems
with mulitple technologies is found between 11-14 years [31], for systems with PV with
batteries is 12 years [30] and for district heating systems as low as 6 years [28]. The
IRR is found to be between 4.1% [34] and 11.9% [32]. Finally, the authors in [29] find
a reduction of 22% in the LCOE of the system compared to the existing system.
3.2
Storage
The Green Energy package for all introduces the role of the aggregator which poten-
tially enables energy communities with storage to have access to balancing markets, and
therefore, provide services to the grid. As a consequence, studying the impacts of energy
communities and storage on the grid is of high importance.
[55] develop an operation strategy model for a community vanadium redox flow bat-
tery to maximize self-consumption and performing primary frequency control.
The
negative economic impact of providing frequency control is found to be less than 1%
[55], while [56] conclude that profitability increases by 14% with low impact on bat-
tery aging.
Additionally, [57] also analyses frequency control services together with
self-consumption and arbitrage, using a mixed integer linear programming for a smart
energy community. [58] and [59], study the effects of EC on the grid. While [58] develop
a Linear Programming optimization model for the sizing of the PV and BESS, which
is then coupled with a power flow analysis; [59] uses a Mixed Integer Linear Program
(MILP) to decide the best Distributed Energy Resources (DER) portfolio, allocation
and dipatch for an EC, while respecting gird operational constraints. It was found, that
when providing peak-shaving the voltage grid loading can be reduced by up to 58%
[58] and that the main strategies to avoid transformer overloads were proper sizing and
placing of batteries and optimally re-distributing PV throughout the community [59].
==PAGE:28==
3.3 Allocation of benefits, costs and fairness
17
Finally, [60], propose a multi-objective optimization problem for the sizing and location
of distributed PV, with the objective to improve energy loss, voltage deviation, voltage
fluctuation, avoiding reverse power flow and voltage violation. It is concluded that to
achieve high penetration of distributed PV a utility-aided installation is needed.
Apart from supporting the grid, energy communities together with storage behind-
the-meter can provide multiple services to the members, such as, self-consumption, peak-
shaving and time-of-use optimization. [48, 37, 49, 50, 51, 59, 52, 30] propose optimization
models to maximize self-consumption. It is concluded that storage can increase self-
consumption between 35-100% [49, 61], and self-suﬀiciency by 0-74% [61, 62], reduce
peak demand by 30% [61] and lower grid exports between 64-94 % [33] with the highest
percentages achieved with shared batteries [63]. Apart from achieving lower grid exports
compared to individual storage, collective storage has an advantage regarding economics,
reducing violations of voltage and current limits [63] and reducing power exchange with
the grid [61, 33]. Additionally, every kWh of community storage installed is 64-94% more
effective at reducing exports from the community to the grid than individual storage [33].
Moreover, storage can generate more value when multiple, stacked services are pro-
vided by the same device or fleet [18]. Therefore, being able to provide balancing services
to DSOs, together with self-consumption or time-of-use optimization increases the prof-
itability of storage systems. Time-of-use optimization or arbitrage, consists on charging
the battery when the prices are low and discharging or self-consuming when the prices
are high. In [64] a MILP model to minimize costs and CO2 emissions to provide arbi-
trage and arbitrage together with peak-shaving is proposed. A similar model is used
by [65] to obtain the optimal electricity sale and purchase strategies with batteries with
a deep analysis of technological progress. It is found that arbitrage of electricity using
batteries is expected to be viable from 2024. [66] find that the most profitable oper-
ation strategies of large-scale batteries in Germany are self-consumption and primary
frequency control. On the other hand, [67] find that the transmission tariff has a very
significant impact on the operational profitability of energy storage systems (ESS) based
on arbitrage. [68] conclude that Li-ion batteries return positive NPV once the invest-
ment costs drop below 250 €/kWh. Similar results are found by [69], with a payback
period around 18 years, concluding that the economic feasibility of battery systems are
highly dependent on direct subsidies. Finally, increasing electricity prices, decreasing
PV feed-in tariffs and falling investment costs of batteries can provide the PV-battery
system more attractiveness [69].
3.3
Allocation of benefits, costs and fairness
An important issue when considering EC is how to distribute the benefits and costs
among the various local players that cooperate. These issues have been addressed mainly
through optimization models and game theoretical approaches.
Various studies tackle the topic of distribution within the energy community through
optimization models. [70] proposes a model considering demand flexible smart homes
==PAGE:29==
3.3 Allocation of benefits, costs and fairness
18
with a shared microgrid and distributed energy resources (DER) for a fair cost distri-
bution using the lexicographic minimax method. A post process sharing method for
prosumers, where in a first stage internal prices are determined by optimization and in
a second stage a compensation methodology is proposed to ensure all members have
benefits is presented in [71]. [72] proposes a model to enhance resilient communities and
avoid PV curtailment and load shedding using decentralized control and the demand
flexibility of each building. With the objective to minimize the electricity costs of the
members, [73] introduces electricity distribution keys determined by the optimization
and based on a defined initial distribution key. In the models presented above, sharing
rules are based on a diversity of parameters: costs, energy exchanged within the commu-
nity [71], willingness to pay of the members [74] or share of investment of the members
[73].
Another approach to distribute the EC benefits and costs are game-theoretic models,
where cooperative approaches provide improved payoffs compared to non-cooperative
ones [52]. [75] proposes a model that firstly solves the design operation to size the assets
and secondly distributes a part of the benefits to the members based on their contri-
bution to welfare. [76] develops a model for a community without direct ownership of
the storage system to enable economic access to the benefits and compares different
market design options based on cooperative and non-cooperative game-theoretic models.
[77] uses theory of Linear Programming Games to distribute benefits to prosumers of
an EC ensuring that no member would do better by leaving the community. A model
for a community energy storage to provide load balancing using a geometric program-
ming problem is proposed in [78]. In [79], a Stackelberg game is presented to optimize
the welfare of the community with multiple objectives to include consumer preferences
such as emission reduction. An optimization model considering a fair distribution of
the reward to the EC, estimating a fair payment for the aggregator operating the EC
and reducing the risk of mismatch between the aggregator’s and members’ goals while
comparing cooperative and non-cooperative game approaches is proposed in [80]. All in
all, the models use different allocation rules: Shapely value [75, 76, 81, 78, 80], nucleous
[76, 81, 80], variance [80] and Nash bargaining solution [52].
The models presented above while scientifically important, often do not represent
reality; where the average household is not equipped with energy management systems
and simulation models. As a consequence, [82] and [83] propose Simulink models where
energy is shared equally between buildings or members, while [84] presents an allocation
based on static and dynamic keys based on the individual participant’s generation and
loads.
Finally, the majority of the models presented requires a substantial amount of mem-
ber’s data. This reliance on consumers’ data raises concerns regarding privacy violation
[85]. Consequently, distributed algorithms which minimize the need for data sharing are
proposed in [77], [86] and [87].
Certain optimization methodologies presented above either can not directly be ap-
plied to shared self-consumption given that they are developed for P2P markets or pro-
sumer based communities; are too complex to apply in a real EC; or distribute energy
based on individual economic values. For the scope of this research, an approach based
==PAGE:30==
3.4 Uncertainty
19
on energy is used considering that consumer’s individual bills raises data privacy issues
and might face more barriers when being implemented in a real setting.
3.4
Uncertainty
Electricity prices, investment costs, load profiles and renewable generation are common
sources of uncertainty when considering energy communities. Not considering uncer-
tainty might result in more optimistic results when comparing to real set ups.
For
instance, disregarding the imperfect foresight and aging impacts of Energy Storage Sys-
tems leads to considerable errors in their valuation [88], while the uncertainties regarding
technology developments, such as increasing life cycles, can increase the revenues of bat-
teries considerably [65].
Given the need to assess the effect of uncertainty on the economic results of EC,
multiple works have analyzed it in the past years. [89] use k-means clustering method
to create scenarios of wind power output for regional energy communities with wind.
The typical wind power scenarios are used on the simulation software, showing that
the scenario method can improve the feasibility and effectiveness of planning. [87] con-
sider uncertainty of prosumer’s load and generation in a Peer-to-Peer market assuming
that uncertainty is stochastic and Laplace distributed. The authors in [90] propose a
chance-constrained programming method to develop the probabilistic constrains which
incorporates the uncertainties of the RES generation and loads for a system with a gas
network, electric distribution network and energy centers. [91] also develop a model for
seeking the optimal size of BESS for wind farm applications with chance-constrained
programming using a Monte-Carlo method to consider uncertainty in wind production.
In [92], uncertainty in electricity prices is considered in a two agent EC where P2P
exchanges are allowed. [93] also consider uncertainty in prices and load in an urban
community energy system with a two-stage stochastic programming approach and pro-
pose scenario vector sampling method for scenario generation. [94] proposes an optimal
load dispatch model for a community energy hub where uncertainties of EVs are mod-
eled using the Monte Carlo simulation and a robust optimization approach is adopted
to deal with the future electricity price uncertainty. Other approaches, do not consider
uncertainty within the model, but assess it a posteriori using representative scenarios
[95]. [72] compare a posteriori approach using a Monte Carlo simulation with a priori
approach using two-stage stochastic optimization. It is concluded that the posteriori
approach leads to an underestimation of the total annual cost.
3.5
Social acceptance
It is often assumed that renewable energies have high social acceptance and therefore the
successful implementation of such projects is straightforward. However, neither public
support nor crucial stakeholder support should be taken for granted [96]. The authors
==PAGE:31==
3.5 Social acceptance
20
in [97] identify five key assumptions as responsible for the lack of genuine understanding
of the dynamics of public acceptance. The five key assumptions are:
• The majority of the public supports renewable technologies.
• Opposition to renewables is therefore deviant.
• Opponents are ignorant or misinformed.
• The reason for understanding opposition is to overcome it.
• Trust is key.
Within literature is has become accepted knowledge that opinion polls show high sup-
port for renewable projects [98, 99, 100]. However, polls are presented as ’factual’ and
accurate with no discussion on their assumptions and their subjectivity. Additionally,
polls show public opinion at a specific moment in time and are unable to reflect the
intrinsic dynamics of public attitudes [97]. Given the assumption that there is a general
support for renewable technologies, it follows that opposition is deviant and therefore,
less legitimate. As a consequence, it is often assumed that opposition stems from igno-
rance of the benefits of the project. This is supported by the observation that public
support increases once the project has been completed and operational [101]. However,
another explanation is that once individuals feel like they ca not further oppose the
project, therefore losing confidence in their ability to effect change, they are less likely
to take action [102]. The premise that the public is hostile towards new technologies
due to ignorance and a lack of understanding, represents a ’deficit’ model. The deficit
model goes hand in hand with the contrast between lay knowledge and expert knowl-
edge. Decision-makers tend to prefer technically framed information [103]. For example,
in large wind projects in the UK, considerable ’hard’ scientific evidence was preferred
rather than reliance on personal experience. Even though evidence seemed to be uncer-
tain or political disagreement was mixed with technical disagreement [103]. Additionally,
expert knowledge is not neutral, but an ideology that is directly accepted by people as
a fact [104]. Assuming that opponents are wrong or ignorant prevents acknowledging
that objectors might have legitimate and valid concerns.
Social acceptance can be divided into three main components: political, market and
community acceptance. Political acceptance is social acceptance on the broadest, most
general level. Public acceptance for renewable energy technologies and policies is high
in many countries, as opinion polls show that a broad majority agrees with the idea of
public support for renewables [96]. Market acceptance is the process of market adoption
of an innovation. Market acceptance focuses on consumers, such as their willingess to pay
for green energy or investors and intra-firm acceptance of renewable energy innovation.
Finally, community acceptance focuses on the specific acceptance of renewable energy
projects by local stakeholders. Is in the community context that the NIMBY (not in
my back yard) unfolds. The NIMBY premise is that the difference between high public
support and the resistance to specific projects is due to the fact that people support
those projects as long as it’s not on their backyard [96]. However, the NIMBY paradigm
==PAGE:32==
3.5 Social acceptance
21
does not take into account the multitude of underlying motivations for public opposition,
such as, physical, contextual, political, socio-economic, social, local and personal aspects
[97].
3.5.1
Energy justice
Community acceptance is often analyzed through the theoretical framework of energy
justice. Sovacool et al. [105] define energy justice “as a global energy system that fairly
distributes both the benefits and burdens of energy services, and one that contributes
to more representative and inclusive energy decision-making”. Thus, energy justice is a
basis for energy decision-making to policy-makers and practitioners to ’provide individ-
uals, across all areas, with safe, affordable and sustainable energy’ [106]. The current
approach focuses on three tenets that are considered as an intertwined whole: distribu-
tional, procedural and recognition justice [106, 107]
Distributional justice relates to the physical and spatial dimension of energy regard-
ing the unequal distribution of resources, risks, and responsibilities [11]. When con-
sidering renewable technologies, examples of distributional justice concerns include the
location of infrastructure, energy carriers, access to energy services, subsidies, invest-
ment costs, ownership, profits, responsibility for action or developmental opportunities
[12, 107]. Additionally, it also investigates how marginalized or less-powerful groups
suffer the negative externalities more than others [12].
Procedural justice calls for transparent, inclusive, non-discriminatory decision-making
processes around energy [11]. This means that all stakeholders involved or affected by
energy decision making need to be able to participate in the process and to be effectively
listened to. It includes: transparency; participation, thus, all actors are given the right
to be able to fully participate in the process; accessibility, all actors have access to all the
relevant information; non-discriminatory; and consultation which considers the different
educational backgrounds and understanding levels of local stakeholders to ensure that
the problem is understood [7]. For the processes to be considered just, meetings must be
accessible, decision-makers must recognize the legitimate contributions of local citizens,
and public input should have some bearing on final decisions [108].
Both procedural and distributional justice are key to the community acceptance of
a project. The perception of fairness and fairness of process are vital for encouraging
engagement and acceptance [109]. However, local communities will respond better to
procedural fairness as opposed to material fairness, since the it might be perceived as
briber [97]. Public engagement is key, for instance, objectors to a wind development
became opponents based on negative experiences of planning processes [97]. Jenkins et
al. [107] propose three mechanisms of inclusion to achieve just outcomes: mobilizing
local knowledge, disclosing information, and representation in institutions. Stakeholder
access to information is able to encourage democracy, minimise corruption, increase
business confidence, foster social stability and is a key component in the mobilisation of
local knowledge [12].
==PAGE:33==
3.5 Social acceptance
22
Finally, recognition justice is concerned with making sure that there is an acknowl-
edgement of inequalities and their fair accounting when devising energy infrastructures
or policies [11]. McCauley et al. [106] imply that recognition justice means that ”in-
dividuals must be fairly represented, that they must be free from physical threats and
that they must be offered complete and equal political rights”.
3.5.1.1
Energy justice within Energy Communities
Energy communities can bring a wide range of benefits, such as, financial benefits for
members, fuel poverty alleviation, skills development, employment opportunities, pro-
moting social cohesion, environmental benefits or addressing inequalities [11, 12, 110,
111, 112, 113, 114, 115].
Procedural and distributive justice elements, such as transparency [11, 115, 116], fair
distribution of financial profits [117] and participation [118] are essential for the success
of an energy community project [119]. Additionally, the distribution of risks, costs and
benefits should be perceived as fair [116, 119]. Given that it can cause intra-community
tensions, particularly when the ownership of assets is shared [116].
Even though energy communities are expected to bring many benefits to the local
stakeholders, it might be that this ”romanticized” idea is hiding some shortcomings
regarding the real impact the communities have in terms of process and outcomes [120].
The assumption that community energy will inevitably entail energy justice is not self-
evident [121]. For instance, in energy communities with smart grids, variable energy
tariffs pose disadvantages for energy vulnerable populations. Low income groups with
low demand flexibility are the most negatively affected consumers, because peak hours
might become too expensive [85]. Moreover, RECs contributing to energy poverty remain
the exception, as they report financial instability, lack of personal and lack of knowledge
to address it [122].
Energy communities do not inevitably entail procedural justice either. Participation
of households did not always lead to higher justice evaluations, Milchram et al. [85] de-
scribes that interviewees questioned the extent to which the team really represented the
neighborhood. Another challenge is that pragmatism prevails, Van Veelen observes that
members of an EC were happy to leave the representatives to take charge [123]. However
this contradicts the principle of participation [115, 116, 123]. Moreover, participation
within EC raises questions regarding the extent to which members are considered equal.
While in certain cases EC can lead to improved equity [115], in other cases it has been
observed that participation within the community is limited to higher socio-economic
groups [124]. A related issue, that raises questions of recognition justice, is that it is
often found that men in the project participate more actively than women, and get
credited for it [11]. Finally, in EC with collective storage insuﬀicient transparency of
the operation of the battery lead to the households to be unable to understand it [85].
Additionally, VPPs being perceived as a ’black box’, together with a full external control
of the VPP, resulted in less engagement from the members[85].
==PAGE:34==
CHAPTER 4
Methodology
The aim of the Thesis is to analyze social acceptance of energy communities with col-
lective batteries. Market and political acceptance are studied through an optimization
model. The different optimization models are described in section 4.1. The economic re-
sults from the optimization model such as savings and energy costs are further analyzed
through a feasibility study, as described in section 4.2. Finally, the methodology used
to analyze community acceptance is presented in section 4.3.
4.1
Model formulation
The optimization model is used to study the benefits of an EC with shared self-consumption
from PV production and a collective battery. As a consequence, the model is used to
obtain the economic savings, self-consumption and self-suﬀiciency of the community and
its members. Three optimization models are developed.
• Main: is a deterministic and aggregated model where the community is modelled
as having one consumer. The model is used to analyze different energy community
set ups and future legislation.
• Stochastic: the main model is expanded to a two-stage stochastic model is devel-
oped to study the effect of uncertainty of prices on the investment on batteries
and the savings of the EC.
• Sharing within the EC: is a deterministic model where individual members are
considered, as well as, different energy sharing strategies. It is used to analyze
how different sharing rules affect the investment on battery as well as the fairness
of benefit and cost sharing between the members of each of the strategies.
4.1.1
Main
The main model is a deterministic model with the objective to minimize the costs of
the EC. Additionally, the energy community is modelled as a whole. Thus, there is no
information regarding the individual consumption of the members. The MILP model is
described below.

The electricity demand can be supplied from the gird, the battery or directly from the

either stored, supplied to the consumers or sold to the grid. Equation 4.4 determines
how much solar energy can be produced considering the installed capacity and the nor-
malized patterns. Equation 4.5 and Equation 4.6 model the storage level. Equation 4.7
establishes that the discharged energy can be either sold to the grid or sent to the con-
sumers, while Equation 4.8 determines that input energy to the battery comes from the
gird or the solar panels. Equation 4.9 represents the maximum and minimum levels
of storage allowed and Equation 4.11 limit the charging and discharging rate. Equa-

==PAGE:37==
4.1 Model formulation
26
the same time. Finally, Equation 4.15 and Equation 4.16 model the energy that goes
from the grid to the community (to demand and storage) and from the community to
the grid (from the storage and PV). The energy that goes from the community to the
grid, can be either sold at psell or evacuated to the grid without compensation (Equa-
tion 4.17). This equation is needed to consider a positive energy bill. Therefore, it is
not allowed that the revenues from selling the excess energy are higher than the costs of
importing from the grid in each billing period, month (Equation 4.18). This constraint
es referenced as positive energy bill throughout the document. Enforcing Equation 4.18
results in different optimal solutions: either the excess energy is sold at price 0 or more
energy is bought from the grid to be able sell more excess energy. It is unlikely that
residential consumers will have the strategic behaviour to import more from the grid in
order to sell more electricity, even more when considering that they might not even have
the information about their electric consumption and solar production. It is more likely
that they will self-consume electricity when they have the need and sell the excess energy
at price zero if needed. Consequently, a penalty is added in the objective function to
ensure that energy will be first self-consumed.
Additional constraints will be included to consider different case studies. The ad-
ditional constraints included in the model are: arbitrage of the battery not allowed
(Equation 4.23), an EC without battery (Equation 4.24) and an EC with a fixed PV
capacity of P in kWp (Equation 4.25). More information about the study cases and how
these constraints are used can be found in section 5.2.

4.1.2
Energy sharing within the EC
Distribution of financial benefits between members of an initiative can cause intra-
community tensions, especially in the case of shared ownership of renewable energy
technologies [116]. Therefore, how energy is shared within the EC is essential to its
success. For the purpose of this Thesis, energy based sharing rules will be considered,
given that they require less information about the members than other types of rules
such as based on equal economic benefits. More information about types of rules can
be found in section 3.3. The deterministic MILP model developed to include members
is shown below, only the changes to the main model are showed.

The first big modification are the energy flows compared to the main problem. When
the members are considered, the energy does not flow directly from the PV or battery
to the grid, but the excess energy is associated with one of the members. The change is
important given that by legislation, the energy does not belong to the EC but to each of
the members. Thus, the economic benefits from selling energy to the grid are individual,
instead of being owned by the EC.
With the new energy fluxes, the energy from the community (battery and PV) and
the grid should supply the demand, plus there is always the possibility that excess energy
is sold to the grid (Equation 4.29). Moreover, the energy discharged from the battery
==PAGE:39==
4.1 Model formulation
28
can only supply the demand (Equation 4.31), while the charging can be from the grid
or the PV (Equation 4.32). The energy going from the grid to the community consists
of the energy supplied to the consumers or to the battery (Equation 4.33).
Finally,
Equation 4.35 is to take into account that a member’s monthly bill can not be negative,
as it was described in subsection 4.1.1. However, in this case it’s the individual bill that
can not be negative instead of the total EC bill. Therefore, the energy that surpasses
that threshold is not paid for (Equation 4.34).
Moreover, in Equation 4.26 the energy sold to the grid needs to be added over all
the members. Equation 4.27 and Equation 4.28 show how the energy from PV and
battery is distributed within the energy community. σt,n represents the percentage of
energy generated or stored in the battery that is sent to each of the members. More
information about the specific rules used in this Thesis can be found in section 5.2.
4.1.3
Model with uncertainty
Electricity prices have been increasing considerably at the end of 2021 and beginning of
2022. Thus, it is evident that electricity prices are a substantial source of uncertainty
when considering EC. Including uncertainty becomes even more relevant when consid-
ering 20 year investment which includes assumptions regarding price developments in
the long term. Therefore, a two-stage stochastic model that considers uncertainty in
electricity prices for an EC with collective PV and battery is presented below. In this
case the first-stage variables are the investment capacities which are decided before the
prices are known. The second-stage variables are the energy flows within the community,
from and to the grid. Only the changes to the main model are included.

4.1.4
Assessment criteria
The results from the optimization models described above will be analyzed using different
common metrics used in self-consumption projects. To calculate the criteria for a specific
period of time, such as year or month, the metrics are summed over the desired period
of time.
• Costs (€): are the costs of importing energy from the grid. It is calculated as the
product between the energy imported from the grid to the demand or the battery
and the prices of buying electricity.

how much energy each member recieves from the battery.
• Revenues (€): economic revenues of selling the excess energy to the grid. It is the
energy sent from the battery or the demand to the grid multiplied by the selling
prices. In the case where the members are considered the calculation takes into

• Total savings (€): the total savings of the EC or member is the sum of the revenues

term and an energy term. The power term is the maximum power a consumer is
allowed to use. For instance, even if the consumer does not use any electricity
during a month, he will still have to pay for the ”right” to consume a maximum
power. The higher the maximum power, the bigger is the cost of the power term.
However, for the calculation of the bill savings, only the energy term is considered.
Consequently, the energy bill savings in percentage are smaller that the total bill
savings.

• Self-suﬀiciency (%): the percentage of the demand that is covered by the energy

has a self-suﬀiciency of 50%, it means that half of the energy is imported from
the grid and the other half from the locally produced energy. In the case where
arbitrage is allowed, the self-suﬀiciency is the energy from the PV to the battery,
assuming that all the energy that is sent from the PV to the battery is then
self-consumed.
