4.2
Feasibility study
A socio-economic feasibility study has been considered for analysing the economic prof-
itability of the project. For this Thesis, the methodology described by [125] is used.
The objective of a feasibility analysis is to evaluate a set of projects based on different
financial indicators such as Net Present Value (NPV), Internal Rate of Return (IRR)
and Payback time.
Discounted Cashflow (DCF) analysis is the standard methodology for evaluating
investment projects. It is based on assessing the costs and benefits of a project over its
lifetime, and making them comparable by discounting. This is done as there exists a
temporal value of money which means that money available at different points in time
has a different value, being higher the closer it is to the present time. The time value of
money is accounted with the discount rate, which reflects the societal cost of providing
finances for a project, or, in other words, it is based on the alternative return that
public investments could generate. For this project a social discount rate of 3% is used
as suggested by the European Commission for socio-economic studies [126].
The costs and benefits are purely based on cashflows related to the investigated
project. Cash flows are the movement of money in and out of a business, a project,
etc. over a specified finite period of time. In this project the incoming cashflows are
the total savings defined in section 4.1.4, while the costs are the operating costs of the
battery and the solar panels. Additionally, the CAPEX are the investment costs on
the batteries and the solar panels and it is assumed that they occur the year before the
start of operation of the project. Two feasibility analysis are conducted, one takes into
account the whole system’s (PV and batteries) costs and savings, while the second one
only looks at the costs and savings of the battery. For the second case, the economic
savings are compared to a case with the same PV capacity but without battery. Then,
==PAGE:43==
4.3 Community acceptance
32
the savings that the battery brings to the EC is the difference between the savings of
the total system and the savings of the EC with only PV. More information about the
economic assumptions can be found in section 5.1.4. The main valuation metrics are
summarized below.
NPV - Net Present Value
The net present value is a central tool in investment analysis. The use of the concepts
described above are used to calculate what a project is worth at the moment of making
the decision. Thus, the NPV is the sum of the discounted cashflows over the evaluation
period T, as expressed below where CF0 are the investment costs, CFt are the net
cashflows and r is the discount rate.
NPV = −CF0 +
T
∑
t=1
CFt
(1 + r)t
(4.64)
IRR - Internal Rate of Return
The internal rate of return (IRR) is the annual effective compounded return rate of a
project or an investment option, i.e. the annual return a project is expected to yield. At
this discount rate, consequently, the NPV would become zero.
PBT - Payback time
The payback time (or payback period) depicts the number of years required to recover
the initial investment through the project’s future cash flows. The shorter the PBT, the
more attractive the project. PBT is popular, because it is transparent, easy to calculate
and not a of of financial knowledge is needed to interpret it. A drawback of PBT is that
it only counts the years until the investment is paid back. Thus, it ignores all cash flows
after that period.
4.3
Community acceptance
The energy justice conceptual tool is adopted to explore concerns regarding the ideas
of justice. This framework is applied through the lens of community energy using data
gathered between September 2021 until February 2022.
The research analyzes how
energy justice is negotiated and contested at community-scale through a focus on on
issues of distributive, procedural and recognition justice.
4.3.1
Data sources
Data was collected through 4 semi-structured interviews with key stakeholders: a mem-
ber of the EC, a non-member, a representative from the town hall who developed the EC
and a representative from the consultancy company who helped develop the EC (will
be referenced as developer). The interviewees were recruited based on purposive and
snowball sampling (member and non-member). Snowballing refers to when an existing
interviewee would forward the researcher’s name to another stakeholder. This limits
==PAGE:44==
4.3 Community acceptance
33
generalizability but is in line with the overall qualitative approach. The interviews were
conducted in Catalan between December 2021 and January 2022. They lasted between
one and one hour and a half, were recorded with the permission of the interviewees, and
transcribed before the analysis. The interview’s guide and the transcribed interviews in
Catalan can be found in Appendix C.
4.3.2
Data analysis
Thematic analysis is the search for themes that are significant to the description of a
phenomenon [127]. It consists on identifying themes through “careful reading and re-
reading of the data” [128]. It’s a type of data pattern recognition in which emergent
themes are turned into categories for analysis. Thus, a coding process implies recognizing
(seeing) an important moment and encoding it (seeing it as something) prior to a process
of interpretation [129].
The method of analysis chosen for this study was an abductive approach.
First,
a deductive approach was used where a priori template of codes are used to identify
themes within the data [130]. Thus, the template was defined before commencing an in-
depth analysis of the data based on the research questions and energy justice theoretical
framework. This involved a template in the form of codes to be applied as a way of
organizing text for later interpretation.
The deductive approach was complemented
with an inductive approach which allows themes to emerge directly from the data [129].
4.3.3
Limitations
It is important to note, that the interviews were conducted 6 months after the main
meetings were held, which might have an effect on how the fairness of those is remem-
bered. Additionally, the member did not attend the meetings, this might impact his
perception of procedural fairness and information transparency. On the other hand, the
non-member did participate on the two first meetings. The developer and the interviewer
knew each other before the interview was conducted. However, given that the context
was in a work environment, trust had already been established prior to the interview.
Overall, given that the research has a clear educational purpose in a context of a Master
Thesis, the interviewees were willing to share their thoughts freely.
Only stakeholders from one village where an EC is being developed were interviewed.
Given that energy justice concerns are highly sensitive to local context [115], only inter-
viewing members from one community raises questions of generalization. However, the
interview with the developer provides a wider view of local contexts across the territory.
Moreover, press posts from different town halls, legal documents, institutional posts and
communicative documentation were analyzed. Additionally, participant observation of
a kick-off meeting with the accepted members of an EC in another location than the one
from the interviews was conducted. Thus, ensuring triangulation of data and improving
the validity, reliability and generalization of the results. It is important to note that
neither of the energy communities considered in this research has been connected to the
==PAGE:45==
4.3 Community acceptance
34
grid. Thus its members have not received any of the benefits of belonging to it. As
a consequence, the perceived distributional fairness is based on an expectation, and it
might change when the EC is operational. Finally, this Master Thesis was conducted
together with the energy company that develops the interviewed ECs. Consequently,
there is a risk of bias towards a more favorable perception of justice. To minimize such
risk, the author made sure to discuss findings with the supervisors and third parties to
ensure higher objectivity.
==PAGE:46==
CHAPTER 5
Case description
In Spain, legislation regarding EC is still in its infant state: only the definition of RECs
has been transposed but its development is still pending. As a consequence, community
actions in Spain are generally driven by the legislation on collective self-consumption
(RD 244/2019). More information about EC in Spain can be found in as mentioned in
section 2.1
A municipal EC will be the focus of this Thesis. This model follows a top-down
approach to implementing a community project: the municipality develops and finances
the project. First, the municipality selects the best public building to deploy a solar
system in a collective self-consumption scheme among public buildings and citizens (such
as the roof of the town hall or a public sports center ) and prepares the legal and economic
model to open participation to citizens. Once the design is clear and the municipality
has access to enough funding to deploy the project, it selects a developer to carry out the
project through a public tender. Consequently, the PV installation is sized based on the
available space. With the PV design the number of members is established, assuming
a part of it will be allocated for self-consumption of public buildings and the rest will
be open to citizen participation, with allocations of approximately 1kWp per member.
After, the municipality organizes information sessions where neighbours can participate
and decide to join the EC. Certain criteria are selected by the municipality to choose
between members in the case there are more neighbours interested than member spots
(such as having the primary residence in the town or being in a vulnerable situation).
To be part of the EC, members must apply for it through a public tender, and if selected,
they need to pay an annual municipal tax covering the costs of the installation. The
allocation is typically granted for 4 years, although citizens are allowed to leave the
energy community at the end of the first year. This fee is calculated based on investment
costs and is always lower than the savings for the members. Finally, all members must
sign a document agreeing on how the energy will be shared, which is sent to the DSO
in order to establish the scheme of energy sharing among community members. These
agreement can be updated up to 4 times a year if necessary.
The EC considered in this Thesis is composed of 32 members. It is assumed that
all members are within a 500 m distance from where the generation is located, in order
to comply with the conditions set by legislation RD 244/2019. Additionally, it will be
assumed that all the members perceive the same electricity prices and sell the excess
energy to the retailer at the same price. However, in reality members don’t need to
belong to the same energy retailer, so they can have different tariffs. In this case, the
EC is a price taker, given that it does not participate in the market, thus, can not
==PAGE:47==
5.1 Data
36
influence it.
The battery is associated with the PV installation as regulated in the
Royal-Decree 244/2019. A li-ion battery will be considered given that they are expected
to dominate the market in the next years [16], have high energy densities and round trip
eﬀiciency [17].
The time horizon used is between October 2020 until September 2021. The time
horizon has been selected considering the availability of consumer data.
5.1
Data
5.1.1
Consumers
Consumer data has been the most diﬀicult data to obtain due to data privacy of EC
members. As a consequence, electricity consumption data was shared by friends, family
and colleagues for the purpose of this project. The final data consists of 30 residential
profile and 2 commercial profiles. The commercial profiles are from two small public
oﬀices where citizens can have access to digital technologies and consultancy services. No
more information about the characteristics of the commercial consumption is available.
On the other hand, the characteristics of the residential consumers can be found below.
Figure 5.1: Consumer characteristics
Figure 5.1 summarizes the main household’s characteristics of the members of the
EC. Half of the members live in houses, while the other half lives in apartments. While
in Spain the majority of the population lives in apartments, being the second country
==PAGE:48==
5.1 Data
37
with the highest percentage of population living in apartments in Europe, this trend is
lower in villages [131]. It is important to note that most of the consumers do not have
electric heating, as it is common in Spain to have a natural gas burner to heat water for
space heating [132]. On the other hand, while most of the older houses also use gas for
cooking, they have slowly been replaced by electric cooking [133].
(a) Size
(b) Number of inhabitants
Figure 5.2: Household characteristics.
Given that half of the dwellings are apartments in cities, the majority of the dwellings
are relatively small, below 100 m2, while all the dwellings above 120 m2 are houses.
Figure 5.2 shows that in the majority of the cases there are 2 occupants per household.
This shows that the data reflects certain intervals of occupants’ age more than others.
However, in Spain more than 95% of household have 3 occupants or less, with Spain
being one of the countries in the EU with the lowest birth rates [134].
It is observed that the total demand per month considerably increases during the
months of winter of 2020 (Figure 5.3).
Last December was not colder compared to
historical climatic data (1960-1990) [135], but it was considerably colder compared to
recent years [136]. Additionally, January was colder than both historical climatic data
and recent years [137].
At first sight, it would not be expected that this surge in
consumption is due to a colder winter, given that most of the consumers do not have
electric heating. However, it is important to take into account that the information
about the characteristics of the dwellings were stated by the consumers. Therefore the
might be a lack of information which reduces the reliability of the data. By analyzing
the data, it was found that a higher amount of consumers, up to 14, might be using
electric heating. Therefore, even if less than half of the households have electric heating,
a colder winter would have a direct impact on the consumption. As a conclusion, the
increase in consumption observed in 2020 is due to a colder winter. For more details, see
Appendix B.1. As expected, the demand shows a peak in the evening, between 20 and
22, when consumers come home. On the other hand, lower demand is observed during
==PAGE:49==
5.1 Data
38
the night when members are sleeping. Finally, for the purpose of this Thesis the demand
is considered inelastic, thus, it does not react to changes in prices.
(a) Total monthly
(b) Average per hour and month
Figure 5.3: Electricity demand.
5.1.2
Buying prices
In Spain, consumers have two options regarding prices for the electricity they consume:
the free market where retailers offer and compete for different prices or the tariff of active
energy invoicing (PVPC as the acronym in Spanish). For the purpose of this project
the PVPC will be used, given that it is the regulated price in Spain. Since June 2021
there is only one residential tariff (2.0 TD) which substitutes all of the previous ones:
default tariff (2.0.A), eﬀiciency with 2 periods tariff (DHA) and electric vehicle tariff.
The new tariff includes 3 price periods, this means that the price is higher, average
or lower depending on the hour of the day. Peak hours are between 10-14 and 18-21,
average periods are between 8-10, 14-18 and 22-24, while low periods are at night from
24-8 [138], as summarized in Figure 5.4.
Figure 5.4: Distribution of time bands (PVPC)[139]
==PAGE:50==
5.1 Data
39
Both the previous and current tariffs are composed by an energy term and a tolls
and charges term, as regulated in the Royal-decrree 216/2014. The energy term com-
prises: day-ahead and intra day prices, ancillary services, capacity payment, marketing
costs and OM and OS financing. Additionally, the SO (System Operator) has to share
this information disaggregated. Tolls and charges, and capacity payments are responsi-
ble for the 3 prices periods. Thus, they increase or decrease depending on the hour of
the day, as seen in Figure 5.5.
(a) Capacity payments [140]
(b) Tolls and charges [141]
Figure 5.5: Selling price’s components responsible for the 3 price periods.
Given that the current tariff has been implemented recently, not enough data is
available for the purpose of this project.
As a consequence, the energy term of the
eﬀiciency with 2 periods tariff (DHA) will be used instead and the current tolls and
charges will be added1. This way, it is possible to take into account how the prices change
based on the hour of the day. It is important to note that by using this methodology, the
changes of capacity payments based on the hour of the day are not taken into account.
However, as shown in Figure 5.6 the contribution of capacity payments to the price is
negligible compared to the tolls and charges.
Figure 5.6: Components of the PVPC 2.0 TD between 2021-06-01 and 2021-09-30[142].
1DHA and 2.0 TD have similar capacity prices per MWH while the rest of the energy term is
calculated using the same parameters.
==PAGE:51==
5.1 Data
40
Finally, since the 16th of September 2021, the tolls and charges were modified with
the objective to reduce the impacts of the increase of electricity prices on consumers
(Royal-Decree 17/2021), as observed in the last values of Figure 5.5. For the part where
the PVPC is created using the previous tariff the political decision will not be taken into
account. Therefore, the higher tariffs will be used as it was intended initially. On the
other hand, for the last 15 days of the simulation the newest charges and tolls will be
used, given that the data was downloaded directly and that it is a small amount of time
of the total simulation. Figure 5.7 shows the final prices used for the model with both
the data downloaded directly and the calculated one. It is observed that the calculated
price and the downloaded one have very similar patterns, with an increase observed
throughout the time horizon due to the increase of electricity prices in the day-ahead
market.
Figure 5.7: Buying prices perceived by the members of the EC between 2020-10-01
and 2021-09-30.
5.1.3
Selling prices
Shared self-consumption energy communities in Spain are being developed through the
legislation for collective self consumption (RD 244/2019). For the purpose of this Thesis,
the simplified compensation will be considered, as this scheme simplifies considerably
the bureaucracy and permitting procedures. More information about the economic com-
pensation of excess energy can be found in section 2.1. Simplified compensation means
that the community is a price taker as it does not participate in the market, and thus
can not influence it. As mentioned before, it is assumed that all consumers perceive the
tariff of active energy invoicing price (PVPC). In that case, the Royal-decree 244/2019
in article 14 establishes that the excess energy produced from the solar panels is sold at
the average hourly price (Pmh by it’s Spanish acronym) minus the balancing costs. The
Pmh is defined in the Royal-decree 216/2014 in article 10 as the average between the
day-ahead market price and the intra-day market price. This data is directly available
==PAGE:52==
5.1 Data
41
in ESIOS and shown in figure Figure 5.8. It is observed that the prices increase con-
siderably in the second part of the time horizon, the same was observed for the buying
prices. This is due to the increase of gas prices and, in a smaller measure, due to the
increase of the CO2 emission certificates in the EU.
Figure 5.8: Monthly average of selling prices from October 2020 to September 2021.
5.1.4
Economic and technological data
Table 5.1 summarizes the main economic and technological data used for the model.
According to [143] the costs of stationary litihum batteries are composed by the battery
pack, Balance of Systems (conatiner, monitors and controls, thermal management and
fire supression), Power Conversion System (inverter, swithces and breakers, Energy Man-
agement System), Engineering Procurement and Construction (project management,
permitting, site preparation, foundation, commissioning) and other developer costs (grid
connection, transport, metering, land, etc.). The battery pack is the main contributor
to the price, accounting for a 50% of it. The costs depend on multiple factors: bound-
aries, application, type of service and size. These vary across studies and are not always
reported. As a result, the uncertainty regarding investment costs of batteries is signifi-
cantly high. As described in the case description, the project is developed by the town
hall, therefore the feasibility study is considered socio-economic. As a consequence, the
discount rate is lower, 3%, than in a private economic study. Additionally, the technical
lifetimes of the assets are considered, as no loans need to be paid back. It is important
to note, that considering a socio-economic feasibility study will make the project consid-
erably more attractive compared to a private one, given that the discount rate is lower
==PAGE:53==

42
and that there are no need for loans. On the other hand, given that the developer of the
project is a public institution, no depreciation of the assets is allowed, thus the taxes
need to be paid fully. A VAT tax of 21% is considered.

5.1.5
PV generation
The PV production was obtained from PVSol, a software tool to design and simulate the
operation of a photovoltaic system. The first step to simulate a PV system is to model
the irradiation.
PVSol obtains irradiation from climatic data based on MeteoNorm
and 37 extra datasets for Spain. The partial steps to calculate the irradiation are the
following:
• Calculation of sun position
• Calculation of extraterrestrial radiation
• Calculation of the geometry of the module area and angle of irradiation, if solar
tracking
• Division of irradiation into direct and diffuse components using the model in [148]
• Calculation of shadowing losses due to distant objects
• Calculation of irradiation on the inclined plane
==PAGE:54==
5.1 Data
43
• Calculation of ground reflection
As a consequence, the data inputs that the user needs to provide are the time frame
for the simulation, location of the installation, orientation (azimuth and elevation), in-
formation about shadowing and albedo (reflexion from the ground). Finally, the global
irradiation onto the inclined plane EGTI is calculated as:
EGTI = EBTI + EDTI + ERTI
(5.2)
Where the EBTI is the direct radiation to the inclined plane, the reflected radiation
ERTI onto the inclined plane and the diffuse radiation EDTI onto the inclined plane. For
the diffuse irradiation the Hay Davies model is used [149]. For the reflected radiation,
an albedo corresponding to residential areas is assumed, given that the solar panels
of the EC are in the roof of municipal buildings. The next step after calculating the
irradiation, is to simulate the electric and thermal behaviour of the PV generator. To
do it, the shading by nearby objects, the module temperature and the current-voltage
characteristic curve are calculated. For the equivalent circuit of a PV cell, PVSol uses
a one-diode model.
The current-voltage characterictic curves are calculated at each
simulation step (hour), after the curves are superimposed to calculate the total field
V-C curve. To model the behaviour of the cell, the user needs to provide geometric data
on nearby objects and PV module data.
Finally, PVSol also includes the simulation of the inverter, which has two functions:
transforming from DC to AC and MPP tracking (maximum power point), which means
constantly adjusting to the point of voltage and current that maximizes the power that
is produced given a temperature and irradiation. Apart from the eﬀiciency of the in-
verter, the model also includes eﬀiciencies of adjusting the MPP, stand-by and night
consummations, and the minimum power at which the inverter supplies energy. Finally,
the PVSol also includes losses in the cables. As a consequence, the solar production
used in this project already includes the relevant losses. More information about the
calculations and assumptions used by PVSol can be found here.
Figure 5.9: Location of solar production.
The solar production data for the optimization model is obtained for the location of
Rupià, a village in the north-east of Spain (See Figure 5.9). The location was selected
==PAGE:55==
5.2 Case studies
44
given that the town hall of the village is considering installing a battery in the existing
EC. The characteristics of the solar field are summarized below.
PV capacity

during the night and longer hours and higher production during the months of summer.

5.2
Case studies
This section introduces the different case studies analyzed. First, the base case, the
legislation cases (arbitrage and positive energy bill) and the case of an existing energy
community are described. This cases use the same data as mentioned in the previous
section. Then, the case that analyses battery investment costs and electricity prices is
introduced. Thus, the electricity prices used for this case are also described. Finally, the
case where energy is shared among individual members is introduced.
5.2.1
Base
The model described in subsection 4.1.1 is used to analyze the benefits of introducing a
battery in an energy community. The base case is one where the community invests in
==PAGE:56==
5.2 Case studies
45
PV and storage simultaneously and arbitrage is not allowed. Additionally, the benefits
from selling excess energy to the grid ca not be higher than the costs of importing. Thus,
the members ca not make profit from their PV production.
5.2.2
Legislation
The model described in subsection 4.1.1 is used to consider how legislation affects the
profitability and operation of a collective battery.
Arbitrage
Until storage legislation is further developed, the current legislation on shared self-
consumption only allows storage when associated with the generation. As a consequence,
the battery is not allowed to charge from the grid and thus do arbitrage. However, a case
where arbitrage is allowed will be considered, given that EC are expected to be an active
member of the energy sector and provide services to the grid. Therefore, it is expected
that the legislation should evolve to allow other use cases apart from self-consumption
for batteries.
Positive energy bill
With simplified compensation, members can not make profit.
Therefore, the excess
energy sold to the grid is subtracted from their energy bill. However, the energy bill can
not be negative. Consequently, if the revenues are higher than the costs of importing
from the grid, part of those will be compensated at price 0. This study case analyses
how this restriction affects the savings of the energy community
5.2.3
Existing EC
Most of the ECs that are being developed do not include storage, given that the market
is still not developed and battery prices are not competitive. As a consequence, it is
expected that in the next years, existing EC will be adding batteries to their system.
Therefore, a case of an EC community with solar panels that wants to add a battery
will be analyzed in comparison to the base case. In this set-up the model described
in subsection 4.1.1 will be run in two stages: the first one the PV capacity will be
determined and the battery capacity will be fixed at 0 kWh. In the second stage, the
PV capacity will be fixed to the solution obtained in the first stage and the model will
be allowed to optimize the capacity of the battery.
5.2.4
Price and cost uncertainty
The market for li-ion batteries is still under development, thus its investment prices are
still significantly uncertain, raging from 300 €/kWh to 1000 €/kWh [143]. As a conse-
quence, it is necessary to analyse those prices further. Therefore, three price cases will
==PAGE:57==
5.2 Case studies
46
be considered: 300 €/kWh, 400 €/kWh and 600 €/kWh. On the other hand, electricity
prices have increased considerably in the last quarter of 2021 and beginning of 2022.
Moreover, it is uncertain how future prices will develop. Consequently, it is essential to
understand the impact of price development and price uncertainty on the investment in
a battery.
To consider price development in the future two cases will be considered: low electri-
city prices and high electricity prices. For both cases, the two-stage stochastic model
and methodologies for scenario generation and reduction presented in sections 4.1.3 and
2.3.3 are used. The difference between the low and high cases is the data used for the
scenario generation: data from 2014 until September 2020 for the low prices and from
October 2020 until January 2022 for the high prices. It is important to consider how
the price data selected for the low scenarios correlates with the consumption data, given
that the data sets correspond to different years. It was observed that all the years have
a correlation between the consumption and the electricity prices between 0.4 and 0.5.
On the other hand, the correlation between the electricity prices for the high prices case
and the consumption, which are from the same year, was 0.2. The lower correlation is
because for that period of time the electricity prices increased considerably while the
consumption did not. The distribution of the data used for the scenario generation for
the high and low cases is shown in Figure 5.11. Buying and selling prices have a similar
distribution given that they are both based on the day-ahead prices.
(a) Buying prices
(b) Selling prices
Figure 5.11: Distribution of electricity prices data used for the scenario generation.
Pre-processing for the selling prices of the low price case was necessary, given that
there is only data published from 2019.
Therefore, the selling price was calculated
according to the RD 244/2019. Thus, the selling price is the Pmh (precio medio horario)
that can be downloaded directly from here, minus the deviation costs that are published
by the system operator (REE) on a yearly basis (Appendix B.2). The calculation was
compared with the data that was available from 05-2019 until 31-12-2020. It was found
that the average difference between both is 0.03 €/kWh and the maximum difference
==PAGE:58==
5.2 Case studies
47
is 1 €/kWh. Given that the difference between both values is negligible compared to
electricity prices, the calculation above is used for the selling prices. On the other hand,
data for the buying prices is available since April 2014, as a consequence, the method
used to approximate older tariffs to the current one, described in section 5.1.2, will
be used. However, the selling prices scenarios will be generated without the tolls and
charges. Tolls and charges are fixed based on the hour of the day, thus they are added
after the scenario generation and reduction.
For the scenario generation the block bootstrapping technique is used to generate
2000 scenarios. The 24 hour blocks are used to take into account the daily correlation
in electricity data. However, electricity prices also have seasonal and weekly correlation
[150]. Figure 5.12 shows that electricity prices have clear different patterns depending
on the day of the week; and a clear different magnitude and variability (Figure 5.13)
depending on the month. Based on the patterns and mean values observed below, three
seasons are considered - summer, spring and winter - and two daily patters -weekday
and weekend. Summer includes the months from June to September, spring from March
to May and winter from October to February.
(a) Weekday
(b) Weekend
Figure 5.12: Hourly average prices between 2014 and 2020 for each month.
As observed above, it is essential to consider the seasonal and weekly correlations
when sampling for scenarios. As a consequence, when sampling a block, it is considered
which day of the week is being sampled for and the season it belongs to. For instance, if
we are sampling for a Sunday (weekend) of August (summer), only days from weekends
in summer will be considered to select from. Moreover, selling and buying prices are
highly correlated given that they are both based on day-ahead prices. To keep this
correlation the same days are sampled when creating scenarios for selling and buying
correlations.
The 2000 scenarios generated are reduced to 50 representative scenarios using the k-
medoids methodology described in section 2.3.3. The Euclidean distance is calculated as
the distance matrix between the scenarios. The scenario reduction is done simultaneously
==PAGE:59==
5.2 Case studies
48
Figure 5.13: Variability of day-ahead electricity prices for each month, data from 2014
to 2020
for selling and buying prices to preserve the correlation between both. The representative
scenarios have a similar distribution as the data used.
Finally, the impact of uncertainty in investment costs and electricity prices will be
assessed for all the possible combination of both. Considering that there are 2 possible
cases for electricity prices (high and low) and two cases for investment cases (300 €/kWh
and 600 €/kWh), 6 analysis will be conducted.
5.2.5
Sharing within the EC
How to share energy within an EC is not trivial and dynamic coeﬀicients arise the ques-
tion of distributional fairness. As a consequence, the model described in subsection 4.1.2
is used to analyze how different sharing coeﬀicients impact the benefits for the commu-
nity and the members individually, as well as, identifying winners and losers from each
strategy. The strategies used are summarized below:
• Fixed sharing coeﬀicients: the members of the EC receive the same percentage of
the energy sent from the PV or battery for each hour of the day. This strategy
represents the current legislation where fixed coeﬀicients throughout the year are
used.
– Based on investment: it will be assumed that all the members participated
with an equal share of the investment of the solar panels.
Thus, all the
members receive the same amount of energy in each hour. The σn,t for each
==PAGE:60==
5.2 Case studies
49
member n ∈N and hour t ∈T is calculated as follows.
σn,t = 1

– Based on consumption: the fixed coeﬀicient will depend on the annual con-
sumption of the member. Therefore, the members with the biggest consump-
tion will receive more energy. The sharing coeﬀicient σn,t for each member
n ∈N and hour t ∈T is calculated based on the member’s demand dt,n

the need to optimize the use of locally produced energy, therefore soon dynamic
coeﬀicients will be allowed. In this case, the energy will be shared based on the
real demand of the members at each time step.

The sharing rules described above will be analyzed in two different context to consider
their impact at several levels:
• Free battery capacity: the model optimizes the installed capacity of the battery.
The objective is to analyze the effect of the different rules on investment decision.
In other words, how the sharing rules impact the size of the battery.
• Fixed battery capacity: the battery capacity is fixed to the capacity obtained in
the base model described in the section above. The objective is twofold: analyze
how the sharing rules affect the benefits of whole energy community; and identify
winners and losers within the members of EC for each of the sharing rules.
==PAGE:61==
CHAPTER 6
Results
This chapter presents the main finding obtained though out this Master Thesis. It starts
with the results of the base case, after they are compared to the arbitrage, existing EC
and positive energy bill study cases. It continues with introducing how electricity prices
and battery investment costs affect the feasibility of the project. Finally, the results
regarding the sharing of energy between individual members is presented.
6.1
Base case
The base case is an energy community where the investment on PV and batteries is
done simultaneously, and arbitrage is allowed.
Considering this set-up, the optimal
investment is 52 kWp of PV and 32 kWh. To understand what the added benefits of the
battery are the results of the PV-battery system are compared to a community with a
PV capacity of 52 kWp and without battery. Table 6.1 summarizes the annual results for
the base case and a reference without battery (No bat), the metrics used in the analysis
are described in section 4.1.4. It is shown that the battery reduced the excess energy
that is exported to the grid by 9,505 kWh/year. As a consequence, the revenues obtained
from selling the excess energy are reduced 674 €/year (21% reduction compared to no
battery). On the other hand, the savings from self-consuming, meaning the energy that
is not bought from the grid because it’s provided by the EC’s PV panels, increased
1,864 €/year (33% increase compared to no battery). This represents an increase in the
energy bill savings compared to the case without battery of almost 9%. Moreover, the
self-consumption and self-suﬀiciency increase 12% and 9% respectively when the battery
is added. The objective value represents the total annualized costs for the EC. The case
without battery has a higher objective value, meaning that the total annualized costs
are higher.

Even if the battery helps the community achieve higher percentages of self-consumption
and self-suﬀiciency, there is a lot of energy that is still exported to the grid. While the
battery reduces the exports by 23%, there are still 30,630 kWh exported every year. It
is important to consider the seasonality of those exports. Figure 6.1 shows the origin of
the energy supplied to the consumer and the destination of the energy produced from
the PV installation. 6.1(a) represents the monthly electricity demand of the EC aggre-
gated per month and the origin of the supply: supplied directly from PV , supplied from
the battery and imported from the grid , all summed over the respective month. 6.1(b)
shows the monthly PV production and where the energy is used - directly consumed by
the community, sold to the grid or charged to the battery - summed over the respective
month. It is observed that the energy provided from the battery to the consumer is rel-
atively small compared to the consumption, it only represents a 10% of the total annual
demand. Additionally, the energy supplied from the grid is much smaller during the
months of summer, while the energy supplied directly from the PV increases. Most of
the energy exported to the grid is during the summer months, when the solar production
is highest roughly double than the one during the winter months. On the other hand,
it is observed that the excess energy (energy sold to the grid) in December and January
is practically zero. This could be the reason why the model does not invest in a higher
battery capacity, as there would not be enough excess energy to store during two months
of the year.
(a) Monthly demand.
(b) Solar production
Figure 6.1: Monthly energy flows.
==PAGE:63==
6.1 Base case
52
Figure 6.2 shows the SOC of the battery for each hour of the day (x axis) and day of
the year (y axis). As expected, the battery charges during the day when there is solar
production and discharges in the night, usually when the demand is higher but also when
the prices also are. Such observation is supported by 6.2(b) where the average charge
and discharge of the battery, together with the average daily demand and PV production
is represented. Additionally, in summer (March - September) the battery is discharged
later, given that consumption peaks are later, but also there is solar production until
later hours. In certain instances the battery waits to discharge on the next morning. This
is because the battery is operated to minimize the costs of the EC. Thus, if the prices
of the morning are higher than the prices of the night, it is better to discharge in the
morning. As a consequence, even if the battery ca not do arbitrage, it can strategically
decide when it’s more profitable to discharge based on electricity prices. However, it
is important to consider that the model assumes perfect foresight.
When operating
a real battery, there is always uncertainty regarding PV generation and prices. Even
though batteries can be operated to consider both PV production and electricity prices,
forecasting models will be needed which will reduce the optimality of the solution.
(a) SOC
(b) Average charge and discharge.
Figure 6.2: Operation of battery.
Figure 6.3 shows the economic indicators of the EC for each month of the simulation:
the revenues from selling excess energy to the grid, the costs of importing electricity from
the grid and the self-consumption savings. The economic metrics are described in depth
in section 4.1.4. The costs show a peak in December and January, this is because there
is a peak in consumption during those months. Secondly, the savings increase through
out the year for both cases - selling excess energy to the grid and self-consuming -,
due to a considerable increase in electricity prices at the end of 2021. Moreover, it is
observed that during the months of June, July and August the revenues from selling and
the costs from buying are equal. This is enforced by the positive energy bill constraint
which ensures the community or members don’t have a negative bill. This indicates,
that in the months of summer, when the solar production is the highest, there is so
much energy to be sold that the monthly revenues are equal to the monthly costs. As
==PAGE:64==
6.1 Base case
53
a consequence, part of the excess energy is sold at price zero to ensure that the energy
bill is zero. This legislative restriction is further analyzed in section 6.2.1.1.
Figure 6.3: Base case: costs, revenues and savings.
To understand better how the annual economic benefits described above impact the
investment decision, a feasibility study has been carried out using the methodology
described in section 4.2. Figure 6.4 shows the accumulated cashflows which represent
the the discounted cashflows summed over past years as described in section 4.2. The
PV-battery system has a NPV of 85,508 €. Additionally, the payback time is 8.5 years
and the IRR is 12.1%.
==PAGE:65==
6.1 Base case
54
Figure 6.4: Accumulated cashflows.
On the other hand, to understand better how the battery adds value to the EC, the
same study has been carried out considering only the investment costs of the battery
and the savings that result from adding a battery. Thus, to calculate the benefits added
only by the battery the savings presented in Table 6.2 for the case without battery
are deducted from the base case. As a consequence, the extra savings that the battery
provides are 1,190 €/year, while the CAPEX are 400 €/kWh. The accumulated cashflows
are shown in Figure 6.4 in orange next to the case where both the PV and battery are
considered. In this case the NPV is 6,023 €, the IRR is 6.9% and the payback time is
13.12. The investment is still feasible, but much less attractive to investors. Moreover,
if the investment costs of batteries increase from 400 €/kWh to 556 €/kWh the NPV
becomes negative.
Given that battery prices have considerable uncertainty, ranging
between 300 to 1,000 €/kWh [143], there is little margin of battery prices where the
investment is profitable.
Furthermore, battery lifetimes have a clear dependency on
the battery use and they can range between 10 and 25 years [17, 144].
To include
how the lifetime impacts on the investment decision, a third feasibility study for the
battery is conducted. In this case, the same economic values as the previous case are
used, CAPEX of 400 €/kWh and savings of 1,190 €. However, it is assumed that the
lifetime of the battery is reduced from 20 years to 12. In this case, the NPV is -1,096 €.
Therefore, the investment is never recovered. Finally, this study has not considered how
the degradation of the battery will affect the feasibility. As a consequence, the values
obtained are optimistic [47].

6.2 Case studies

6.2
Case studies
In the previous section the results for the base case, an EC which invests in PV and Li-
ion batteries at the same time and arbitrage is not allowed, are presented. However, EC
in Spain are a recent innovation and both legislation and different set-ups are likely to
develop in the next years. As a consequence, this section has the objective of analyzing
different developments of ECs, as well, as uncertainties in prices and costs.
6.2.1
Annual results
This section compares the annual results for the base case, a case that allows arbitrage
and a case where the investment in the battery is done after the investment in the PV.
All cases description can be found in section 5.2.
Investing first in PV and then adding a battery, results in a higher PV installation
and a smaller battery. This indicates that adding a battery to an EC reduces the need
for PV installation. This is particularly interesting for cases where the space to put
PV panels is limited.
Additionally, it also indicates that the investment of PV and
batteries simultaneously is a more profitable investment than PV. As a consequence of
a smaller battery and higher PV production, the the existing EC case exports 10,910
kWh more to the grid. Additionally, as less energy can be stored for later consumption,
the self-suﬀiciency and self-consumption percentages are also lower. On the other hand,
the existing EC case has higher revenues from selling excess energy to the grid but lower
savings from self-consumption. However, overall it achieves higher total savings than
the base case. Finally, the objective values for both cases only differ by 39 €.

6.2.1.1
Positive energy bill
This section analyses the impacts that the positive energy bill constraint has on the
energy community.
Postive energy bill means that the members or in this case the
energy community can’t make profit from the PV production. Therefore, the monthly
bills can’t be negative. The positive energy bill has a direct impact on revenues, and
thus on the feasibility. Figure 6.5 shows the extra annual revenues the community could
have if the bill was allowed to be negative. The EC’s base case could have 113 € more
savings if the positive energy bill was not enforced, which represents an increase in NPV
of 2,000 €. However, 113€ split among 32 members per year represents only an addition
of 3.5 €/year for each of the members, which is a very small contribution individually.
==PAGE:68==
6.2 Case studies
57
Both the base and arbitrage cases have similar extra revenues. However, the existing EC
case could have three times more savings than in the other two cases. This is because
the existing EC case has a higher PV production and a lower battery. Thus, less energy
can be stored to be used later. As a consequence, more energy is sent to the grid without
being economically paid for than in the other cases .
Figure 6.5: Extra savings if a negative energy bill was allowed.
6.2.2
Battery operation
Only the battery operation for the arbitrage case is presented, given that the rest of the
cases do not experience significant changes regarding the battery operation.
6.2.2.1
Arbitrage
Figure 6.6 shows that the battery charges during the early hours of the morning to
discharge during the peak hours around 8-10 in the morning. Thus, allowing the EC to
save money during hours where the prices are lower, while indirectly helping the system
by shifting consumption from peak hours to low demand hours. After, the battery is
charged throughout the day with the excess energy from the PV and discharged in the
night.
This indicates that the battery has more cycles than in the base case where
arbitrage is not allowed, which has a significant impact on the lifetime of the battery.
==PAGE:69==
6.2 Case studies
58
Figure 6.6: Average charge and discharge per hour: base and arbitrage cases.
Additionally, the battery is operated much more dynamically depending on the
month. With months like July having much less storage than December. Figure 6.7
shows the SOC of the battery for the whole year. The morning charging cycle, when
arbitrage is performed according to Figure 6.6 is mostly seen in winter when the demand
is higher. In summer the battery is less used and usually only one cycle is performed.
The differences between winter and summer could be explained by a higher demand in
winter and more solar production that starts earlier which then can supply the morning
consumption.

(b) Arbitrage
Figure 6.7: SOC.

6.2.3
Electricity prices and battery investment prices
This section presents the results for the two-stage stochastic model that considers un-
certainty within prices. As mentioned in section 5.2 two cases of prices are considered
(low and high) and three investment costs are considered (300, 400 and 600 €/kWh).
Figure 6.8 shows the invested capacity for the PV in kWp and for the battery in
kWh for each of the cases mentioned above. First of all, when the costs of the battery
are 600 €/kWh in any of the cases there is no battery installed. This indicates that
at a price between 400 and 600 €/kWh the battery is too expensive to be of added
value. On the other hand and as expected, the cheaper the battery is, the more capacity
is invested. When the prices are decreased 25%, from 400 to 300 €/kWh, the battery
capacity increases 200% for the high electricity prices and 40% for the low electricity
prices. Thus, battery prices have a significant impact on the attractiveness of investing
on a battery. On the other hand, the bigger the installed capacity of battery is the
smaller is the PV installation. Thus, adding a battery in an EC reduces the need for
PV production. Finally, higher electricity prices result in bigger PV installation, given
that there is more added benefits to self-consume more.
Figure 6.8: Results model with uncertainty: invested capacities.
6.9(a) shows the distribution of the objective values for all the scenarios in each of
the cases analysed. There is a clear difference between the high and low electricity prices
cases, in both value and distribution with the high prices scenarios objective values being
more spread than the low cases. Additionally, the cases with 300 €/kWh as CAPEX
for batteries, achieve the lowest objective values in both the high and low prices cases
respectively. The reasons are twofold: the battery costs are lower, thus decreasing the
==PAGE:71==
6.2 Case studies
60
total CAPEX, but also a higher battery capacity allows to store more energy and reduce
the costs of importing from the grid.
(a) Objective value
(b) Savings
Figure 6.9: Economic results model with uncertainty.
Furthermore, 6.9(b) represents the savings achieved by the EC for each of the sce-
narios in each of the cases analysed. In this case, the high electricity prices achieves
the highest savings given that costs for both importing from the grid and selling excess
energy to the grid are higher. Additionally, the investment costs of batteries and as
a consequence, the capacity installed of battery, have a significant impact on the sav-
ings. For the case of low electricity prices, when the investment costs decrease from 600
€/kWh to 400 €/kWh, the savings increase approximately 1,800 € annually. Addition-
ally, for the high prices case, when the investment costs decrease from 400 €/kWh to
300 €/kWh, the savings for the EC increase 1,000 € per year.
6.2.3.1
Solution evaluation
This section assesses the stochastic solution using the methodology described in section
2.3.4. The EVPI represents the maximum amount a decision maker would be ready to
pay in return for complete and accurate information about the future. Table 6.6 shows
that the decision maker is more willing to pay for accurate information the higher the
electricity prices are and the lower the costs of the battery are. For battery investment
costs of 600 €/kWh the EVPI indicates that there is no added value to have accurate
information about the prices. In this case, the investment prices are too high and there
is no battery installed. Consequently, energy is sent to the members and exported to
the grid when there is solar production, irrespective of the prices. The VSS shows the
benefit of using stochastic programming compared to using expected values. The higher
the electricity prices and the investment costs of battery the more benefit there is to use
stochastic programming.

6.3
Sharing within the EC
This section presents the results for the EC when individual members are considered.
Three sharing rules are compared: dynamic coeﬀicients where the energy is distributed
based on the hourly demand of each of the members (referenced as dynamic); fixed
coeﬀicients where each member receives the same amount of energy at each hour based
on their annual consumption (referenced as fixed); and fixed equal coeﬀicients where all
the members receive the same amount at each hour (referenced as fixed equal). Moreover,
two investment cases are considered. The first one, is a direct comparison with the base
case, where the investment in PV and battery are fixed (referenced as fixed capacities).
The second case, allows the model to optimally invest in PV and battery capacities
(referenced as optimal capacities). More information can be found in section 5.2.
The results are presented form the community level - which benefits and costs does
each strategy entail for the community - and at the member level - how the different
strategies affect the benefits for the members.
6.3.1
Community perspective
First of all, as expected the most ineﬀicient rules regarding the use of self-generated
energy are the fixed and fixed equal coeﬀicient models. When energy is sent without
considering whether it can be consumed, a considerable share of the sent energy is sold
to the grid. Table 6.7 shows clearly this observation in the case where the capacities
are fixed. The premise that all the sharing rules have the same capacities installed,
enables to observe how the sharing coeﬀicients affect the performance and use of the
energy. For instance, the dynamic coeﬀicients export 8,000 kWh less per year compared
to the fixed coeﬀicients and 13,000 kWh/year less compared to the fixed equal coeﬀicients.
Consequently, the dynamic coeﬀicients achieve higher percentages of self-consumption
and self-suﬀiciency. On the other hand, the fixed equal coeﬀicients achieve the lowest
percentages, given that the energy that the members receive is the same for all of them,
regardless of their consumption. As a consequence, members with high consumption
==PAGE:73==
6.3 Sharing within the EC
62
receive the same energy as consumers with little consumption who will have to export
it to the grid.
Secondly, the effect of the sharing rules on the investment decisions is observed in
Table 6.7 referenced in the table as optimal capacities. The fixed coeﬀicients rules have
a significant effect on the battery size, reducing the installed capacity by half in the
fixed case and by 6 in the equal fixed. The fixed sharing rules enforces that energy is
sent ineﬀiciently, as a consequence, energy that is stored in the battery would eventually
be sold to the grid. It follows that investing on a big battery is a waste of resources.
Consequently, that the more ineﬀicient the sharing is, the less attractive is the investment
on a battery. On the other hand, in the dynamic coeﬀicients case, the battery installed
is higher than in the base case (32 kWh). This could indicate that once the individual
demands are included, a bigger battery is preferred to help the EC distribute the benefits
among the members.

Regarding the economic results, Table 6.8 shows that the dynamic coeﬀicients is
the sharing rule that yields the highest savings in both cases.
This is because self-
consumption is on average more profitable than selling the energy to the grid, as buying
prices are always higher than selling. Therefore, given that the dynamic coeﬀicients rule
aims at sending energy when it can be consumed, it follows that the economic benefits
are higher. On the other hand, the fixed equal coeﬀicients achieve the lowest savings for
the same reasoning explained before. As energy is sent ineﬀiciently, it has to be sold to
the grid which is economically less beneficial than self-consuming. Finally, in the fixed
capacities case, it is observed that the revenues from selling excess energy are higher in
the case of fixed coeﬀicients compared to fixed equal coeﬀicients. However, the energy
sent to the grid is much higher in the latter. This is because the same energy is sent to
all consumers regardless of their consumption. Consequently, more energy is sent to the
grid by the members with low consumption. Who at the same time, are more likely to
have to sell their energy at price 0 because of the positive energy bill restriction.

6.3.2
Member perspective
The results presented in this section are for the case of fixed capacities, for a PV installa-
tion of 32 kWp and a battery of 32 kWh. This is done in order to compare the different
sharing rules easily in a shared environment.
(a) Total Savings
(b) Energy bill savings
Figure 6.10: Savings for the members.
First, it is important to take into account that members have more savings the more
energy they receive, as they will either self-consume it or sell it and both actions have
economic benefits. However, the savings will differ depending on how much energy is
self-consumed, given that self-consuming is more beneficial economically than selling
excess energy as buying prices are always higher than selling prices.
Figure 6.10 shows the distribution of savings of the members for each of the sharing
rules. Figure 6.10(a) represents the number of members that have certain savings in
==PAGE:75==
6.3 Sharing within the EC
64
euros, while Figure 6.10(b) shows the number of members that save certain percentage
of their initial energy bill.
The distribution between both graphs is completely different. On the one hand, for
the fixed equal coeﬀicients, the total economic savings are the most similar among each
other, with the majority of the members having savings between 200 and 250 €/year.
This is because the same amount of energy is sent for all the members. On the other
hand, these economic savings represent a very different percentage of the energy bill
for each of the members, given that they have different amounts of consumption. For
instance, it is seen that one of the consumers achieves a 100% savings on their energy bill,
this means that by joining the EC this member would have its full energy bill covered.
This member has a very small consumption compared to the rest of the members. While
this member has a 0.61 % of the total community consumption, they1 receives 3% of the
community’s energy in the case of fixed equal coeﬀicients.
From the monetary perspective, the sharing rules based on demand - dynamic and
fixed - have a member that has savings higher than 1,200 €, while the average is around
300 €. This is because this consumer represents 13% of the community’s demand. This
is not the case for the fixed equal, as the sharing is not based on the member’s demand.
Figure 6.11: Average share of energy received by members.
As mentioned before, member’s savings are based on how much energy they receive.
To understand better the obtained results, it’s necessary to look at how energy is shared
between the members. Figure 6.11 shows the distribution of members based on the
hourly average percentage of energy the member’s receive. For the dynamic and fixed
coeﬀicients, the distribution is similar given that they are both based on consumption.
The outliner that receives the highest amount of energy, is the member that has a 13%
1They/their is used in this section as the gender neutral pronoun.
==PAGE:76==
6.3 Sharing within the EC
65
of the community’s energy and also received higher annual savings. On the other hand,
the member that receives less energy is because they has a lower consumption.
For the case with fixed equal coeﬀicients, the economic benefits of selling excess
energy to the grid are similar among members, with the majority selling between 75 and
100 €. For the dynamic and fixed cases, the member that has higher revenues, is the
member with higher consumption. On the other hand, this member is the one that has
the less amount of revenues in the case of fixed equal coeﬀicients, given that as their
consumption is higher they can self-consume almost all the energy that is sent. Thus,
there is no energy left to be sold to the grid.
Figure 6.12: Member’s revenues.
As expected, self-consumption and self-suﬀiciency are higher for the dynamic coef-
ficients sharing rule, as energy is sent to the members when they can consume. Ad-
ditionally, the members have similar self-consumption percentages, as the energy sent
is directly proportional to their hourly consumption. On the other hand, for the fixed
equal coeﬀicients as the energy sent is not based on demand, both the self-consumption
and self-suﬀiciency are very different between members. For instance, the member that
has the highest consumption achieves a self-consumption of 100% as the energy sent is
significantly lower than the consumption. On the other hand, even if the fixed coeﬀi-
cients are eﬀicient, as it considers the annual consumption when sending energy, the
member’s achieve similar percentages of self-consumption compared to the fixed equal
case. Regrading self-suﬀiciency, the dynamic model achieves higher values, given that
member’s hourly demand is considered. Additionally, it ensures that member’s have a
minimum percentage of self-suﬀiciency of 30%. On the other hand, the member that
has the highest consumption achieves the lowest self-suﬀiciency with the equal fixed co-
eﬀicients, as the energy sent is relatively small (3% of the community’s) compare to the
demand (13% of the community’s).
==PAGE:77==
6.3 Sharing within the EC
66
(a) Self-consumption
(b) Self-suﬀiciency
Figure 6.13: Member’s energy performance.
To understand better the differences between members and the winners and loser
of each strategy, four representative members are presented and analysed further: con-
sumption during solar hours (Member 1), consumption in non-solar hours (Member2),
high annual consumption (Member 3) and low annual consumption (Member 4). Fi-
gure 6.14 shows their average consumption during the day.
Figure 6.14: Average consumption selected members.
Table 6.9 summarizes the savings and percentage of energy that each member re-
ceived from the EC (PV or battery) for each of the selected members and sharing rules.
==PAGE:78==
6.3 Sharing within the EC
67
First, the dynamic and fixed sharing rules distribute energy based on the consumer’s
demand. On the other hand, the fixed equal rule distributes the same energy to all the
members. However, even if members receive the same amount of energy, their savings
are different. This is because the members can either self-consume the energy or sell
it to the grid with self-consuming being economically more beneficial given that buying
prices are always higher than selling prices. As a consequence, member 3 achieves the
highest savings given that their2 consumption is the highest. Thus, when energy is sent,
it can almost always be self-consumed. On the other hand, Member 4 has a very low
consumption, so when energy is sent, most of the time it needs to be sold. Therefore,
their savings are the lowest.
Member 1 has more savings with the equal fixed rule. This is because this is the case
where more energy is sent (3.1 %). On the other hand, the dynamic rule sends more
energy than the fixed, this is because this member has a higher consumption during
solar hours (Figure 6.14). Thus, when there is solar production more energy is sent
to them. On the contrary, member 2 consumes mostly during hours where there is no
PV production. As a consequence, the dynamic sharing rule sends less energy than the
fixed rule because the hours where there is production the member is not at home and
consumers that are at home during those hours are prioritized.
Member 3 receives much less energy with the fixed equal rule. As member 3 has the
highest consumption of the EC, accounting for 13% of the total annual demand, when
sharing the energy equally between members, member 3 has 1,000 € less savings than
with the other sharing rules. On the other hand, member 4 is the member with less
consumption in the EC, accounting for only 0.61% of the annual consumption. As a
consequence, this member receives 5 times more energy in the case of the fixed equal
coeﬀicients compared to the other rules. However, the savings barely double. This is
because, the member can not use all the energy and thus has to sell it. However, their
savings ca not be higher than the bill, as a consequence, this energy will be sent to the
grid without any economic compensation.
