==PAGE:79==
6.4 Community acceptance
68
6.4
Community acceptance
The energy community discussed in the following section is a top-down initiative. This
means, that the municipality, with the support of a supra-municipal institution, devel-
ops and finances the project. Therefore, the siting of the solar panels is on municipal
and public buildings. The EC is a shared self-consumption between the town hall and
certain selected neighbours. The town hall makes a step towards carbon neutrality by
investing in solar panels for their own consumption. However, they decide to oversize
the installation with the purpose of including neighbours. The objective of the EC is
threefold: provide green energy for the public buildings, share the benefits with the
neighbours and target energy poverty households.
Neighbours can join the EC through a tendering process and if accepted, they are
part of the EC after the payment of a fee. The payment of the fee is required by law
given that public entities are not allowed sell kWh. However, they can give away the
rights to use public infrastructure for private purposes through the payment of this fee.
The procedure is comparable to bars using public spaces for their terraces, as explained
by the town hall representative:
We are talking about using a public good. It’s like a bar that has a terrace
on the street. The street is property of everyone, but the bar wants to put
tables and chairs. [To do so], he has to pay a fee to the town hall for the
private use of the public good. In this [EC] case , the neighbours do the
same, because they make a private use of the solar panels that the town hall
owns. - Town hall representative
The fees are renewed annually with a maximum of four years according to legislation.
This means, that the members need to pay the fee every year but cannot be part of the
EC longer than four years. On the other hand, this method allows the members of the
EC to leave the community at any point. The members can choose between two different
fees: either to have access to 1 or 0.5 kWp of the municipal PV installation. Therefore,
there is a fixed number of citizens that can join the EC as the PV installation has a
fixed number of kWp. Finally, the fee is calculated based on the annualized costs of the
investment, maintenance and other bureaucratic costs, such as, the access to a platform
where the individual consumption and PV production are shown. More information
about the case description can be found in section 5
The results regarding community acceptance are presented within the theoretical
framework of distributional, procedural and recognition fairness. Distributional fairness
answers the question where are the injustices?; recognition who is ignored?; and pro-
cedural is there fair process?. More information about energy justice can be found in
section 3.5.1.
==PAGE:80==
6.4 Community acceptance
69
6.4.1
Distributional justice
Distributional justice is concerned with the distribution of benefits, ills and responsibili-
ties [107]. The municipal energy communities discussed here are particularly interesting
for families and households that do not own their property, who do not have the financial
means to invest privately or are curious but are risk averse and would prefer to see the
benefits of green energy before investing themselves.
It’s perfect because you don’t have the obligation to invest. [...] I’m renting,
and I’m not sure I’ll be in the same house, and right now I wouldn’t have
the money to make the investment. [...] It’s a way to start getting into this
sector without having to make the investment. - Member
I think we need to find an equilibrium between those families who can self-
consume or install in their own roof, and the rest. And the public roofs need
to be for those families who can’t put solar panels. - Town hall representative
Even though the model is interesting for a certain sector of society, the member selection
criteria did not take that into account. The regulating bases for the tendering set three
main requirements to be part of the EC [151]:
• The member needs to be 500 m within the generation point. This is done to comply
with the legislation about shared self-consumption (Royal decree 24/2019).
• If the request is from a company, it has to be a small or medium-sized company.
• The applicant needs to fill in the application form and send two electricity bills.
In the case where there are more requests than positions in the EC, the regulating bases
considered three prioritization rules. Certain villages added extra prioritization rules,
for example, that residential consumers have priority [152].
• The household needs to be a first residence.
• Citizens who requested the quota of 1 kWp might be reduced to 0.5 kWp, to include
more members.
• By order of arrival of the application forms.
It is clear from the prioritization rules that the objective of the town hall is to share
the benefits with the highest amount of citizens possible.
The interest, at least for the town hall, was to share the 22 kWp with small
pieces, to have the maximum amount of beneficiaries. -Town hall represen-
tative
==PAGE:81==
6.4 Community acceptance
70
However, it is questioned by one interviewee whether the benefits for the members
are enough and who benefits the most from the project. On the one hand, 1 or 0.5 kWp
are believed to be a very little contribution to the individual, both economically and
regarding their environmental footprint. Thus, the EC is not considered as the right
policy to promote renewable energy at an individual level. This is supported by the
developer, who observes that the quota that is preferred is the bigger one of 1 kWp.
This could indicate that the citizens would prefer even bigger shares if that was allowed.
The energy and economic saving [for the members], it’s not really there.[...]
But you [as an individual] joining an EC, the savings you would get and your
contribution to the environment is very small. Because you have to share
with a lot of people. As an idea I think its very good, but it’s not eﬀicient
[as a means to promote green energy]. - Non member
The town hall agrees that citizens who were looking for a substantial contribution
to the reduction of their CO2 emissions or considerable savings, were not convinced to
join the initiative. Additionally, it was diﬀicult to motivate citizens to join for such a
small economic saving.
- [Citizens who] would look for a definitive solution to be self-suﬀicient and
disconnect from the company, [...] saw this proposal as a middle solution.
[The EC] is more about being part of a community, participating, obtaining
a bit of savings, and maybe it didn’t fulfil the expectations of those profiles.
- Town hall representative
On the other hand, the project is not perceived by the same interviewee as an energy
community given that the main benefits are for the town hall. It’s seen as a town hall
promoting their own self-consumption. Moreover, during one of the meetings distribu-
tional fairness concerns were expressed regarding the sharing of energy during summer.
An attendee observed that most of the energy was given to public buildings, however in
summer when the production is highest, those buildings have lower consumption. Thus,
most of the energy during those months would be sold to the grid.
This is promoting [green] energy for the municipal buildings. I don’t see
it being an energy community. [...] It’s the town hall that promotes green
energy [for themselves] and then they have some excess energy. If they would
have said let’s get some PV and put families. I would see it more [as an EC].
- Non member
Additionally, the quota that is required by law, can be perceived as indirectly financing
the municipal installation or paying for the electricity of households with energy poverty.
This raises the question whether the communication about the purpose of this quota was
done thoroughly and will be discussed further in the next section.
==PAGE:82==
6.4 Community acceptance
71
If you want to promote green energy, you are not doing it here [joining the
EC]. You are financing the solar panels of the town hall some years, it’s like
paying a rent. - Non member
A colleague went to the meeting in (another village where they have an EC)
and told me: I will not join, because I’m financing the electricity of someone
who can not pay. - Non member
Even though there are concerns regarding who benefits from the project. The EC is
regarded as having a positive impact on the community as a whole. Both in terms of
promoting renewable energies by the town hall, considering energy poverty and promot-
ing citizens to invest themselves.
At the town hall level, I think it’s very good that they invest in this. First
to promote these energies, but if there is part of the people who financially
can’t pay [and they can join the EC]. Then perfect. - Non member
The fact that the administration believes in it, is that we are like a model. If
something works for the administration [public organitzations], it’s possible
that it works in a residential or business context. So, here we saw that, a
calling effect [people getting more and more interested about solar panels]. –
Town hall representative
Additionally, there is a proposal to develop the project further and include another
PV installation only for businesses.
According to the town hall, this is expected to
promote local businesses in the village. Moreover, one of the main externalities of the
project is the knowledge that has been created while developing it.
It is important
to consider that this municipality together with three more, were the first ones to im-
plement the pilot project of a municipal EC in Catalonia. The project was done in
collaboration with the Diputació de Girona, a supra-municipal institution that designed
the community set-up.
I understand that it was like a win-win [for the town hall and the Diputacio
de Griona]. The diputació used 4 town halls that were “lab rats” to see
when we try to set up the model which real diﬀiculties we find [...] and the
Diputació were able to create a model and now have it easier to expand it
in other municipalities. - Town hall representative
Therefore, the experience of these municipalities is being used to develop more EC using
the same set-up, specifically in 65 more villages [153]. Moreover, the municipality is very
eager to share their knowledge by participating in workshops and talks or contributing
to this research by discussing their experience and providing contacts for the interviews.
Finally, the project is also expected to alleviate the economic burdens of the electricity
bill on households with energy poverty that are within the 500 m requirement. However,
the regulating basis did not take into account the specific needs of the households with
==PAGE:83==
6.4 Community acceptance
72
energy poverty. It is stated in the legal ordinance that all members of the EC have to
pay the fee. However, the town hall has solved this problem temporarily through social
services who will pay for the fee. Regardless of the background of the members, everyone
needs to pay the fee as the town hall ca not give away the private use of a public good
for free. However, in the future, the ordinances will include a section to allow households
with energy poverty to receive a tax bonus and pay the legal minimum.
6.4.2
Procedural justice
Procedural justice concerns access to the decision-making processes [107]. Figure 6.15
shows the timeline of the project since the first meetings where scheduled until current
day. It is observed that there were 3 meetings scheduled: the first and second ones were
focused on explaining the set-up of the EC, what it consists of and answering all the
questions the citizens might have; while the third one was to explain the project again,
but also guide the citizens through the process of application.
Figure 6.15: EC timeline.
To reach out to the citizens the town hall used social media, directly talking to the
citizens and a magazine that is published biannually and is quite popular in the village,
mostly among older citizens. Overall, the consensus of the interviewees is that the town
hall communicated the project properly to the citizens.
==PAGE:84==
6.4 Community acceptance
73
Being a small village you you find out [about the project] very easily. You
talk to the other parents from the school, and people talk about this. Also
the town hall did a lot of communication. Instagram, social media. There
is a magazine that they give every 6 months and many things come up. I
would say that between people who check social media, then that’s half of
the village, and the other half that’s older people, they found out trough
magazines. Mostly because [the magazine] is very popular and well also just
talking to people. - Member
However, all the interviewees agreed that the village is not aware that such a project
is being developed.
Mundaca et al.
find that informal information channels added
legitimacy to consultation processes and facilitated participation [12]. In this case both
formal and informal channels were used, however participation was limited. Nevertheless,
an increase of interest after the tendering process was closed has been observed by the
town hall and is expected to continue increasing once the project is working and the
members start receiving benefits.
Thus, the town hall has observed a calling effect.
Once the neighbours heard from each other about the EC, more citizens have contacted
the town hall. Unfortunately, the tendering process was closed and they will have to
wait until a new one is opened.
People don’t know [about the EC]. I mean, in the web page it’s there and
there is the information. But people don’t check it. I think more talks are
needed in the village. Not only in this [village] but others. Maybe if you
have a talk every year people would not go. But maybe if you try to make
more, to give more information to people. - Non member
On the other hand, in the first meeting there was little attendance.
One of the
interviewees saw it as a lack of interest from the town hall to communicate the project,
given that the amount of kWp they can share is fixed. Therefore, EC raise questions of
both distributional and procedural fairness simultaneously.
I said, there is not that many people. And the councillor tells me: ”it’s not
in our interest, because after we couldn’t. [...] because we don’t want many
people”. Because of course what you [the town hall] want is for the municipal
buildings and energy poverty, and whatever is left [for the rest]. And that’s
not promoting PV. - Non member
Therefore, the communication is directly dependent on the town hall and thus their
interests can prevail which blends distributional and procedural justice concerns.
As we do all the communication to the members through the town hall, and
if the town hall is not motivated, then there is not that many people who
get to know about it. One of the town halls, had an interest to keep more
generation for themselves, for their own consumption, so it was not in their
interest that a lot of people joined. [...] I believe that the interest [of the
==PAGE:85==
6.4 Community acceptance
74
members] is very linked to how you connect with people. And it’s clear in
those cases, we can see it with one town hall where they are super motivated,
the energy transition is super important for the municipality. - Developer
As mentioned above, the two first meetings focused on explaining the set-up of the
community and the benefits for the members. However, one of the interviewees consi-
dered that the meetings did not put the focus enough on climate targets at an individual
level and that there was a missed opportunity to inform about green energy.
The meeting was focused on we have this project that is the EC and as a town
hall we decided to go this way. But for the rest of the users, they haven’t
focused on let’s promote people to put green energy.
I think its what’s
missing. Maybe it was not the objective of the project. - Non member
Additionally, during the meetings the questions were primarily about the set-up of the
community and benefits for the members. This questions the assumption that EC will
put the citizen in the middle of the energy transition and make them active participants.
It is observed that when those policies are brought to local contexts, practicalities such
as savings tend to be the main concerns for the citizens. This observation interlinks
with the motivations of citizens to be part of an EC. In that effect, only the interviewed
member considers that the main motivation to join the EC is environmental. On the
other hand, the developer and the non member consider that the main motivation is
economic, while the town hall representative and the developer as a second motivation,
think it’s to be part of a community. Additionally, all the interviewees believe that
the high energy prices are a positive incentive to be part of an EC. Thus, reinforcing
the assumption that the main motivation is economic. Fina et al. reached a similar
conclusion, where despite the multiple motivations for energy community participation,
a degree of economic viability is always important [84].
It seems that the meetings did not achieve the objective of solving the questions of
the members and a lot of concepts regarding the role of the town hall, the nature of the
EC or the purpose of the fee are not clear.
So it was not very clear the savings that you would have every month. They
just told you you had to pay every month depending on the kw that you
had.
And maybe it was not clear.
[…].
I think there was missing a bit
of information, although sometimes people if there is too much information
then they don’t pay attention. - Non-member
I think that the participatory part [is not clear]. Am I a client of the commu-
nity? How far is the limit? Can the next village join? Or can other types of
energy join? I think it’s this, like we made a community, but we could have
been a copperative, or an association. Why is it a community? If there is
a regulation or founding bases. We haven’t participated on that, or haven’t
seen them - Member
==PAGE:86==
6.4 Community acceptance
75
Additionally, citizens have limited access to experts who can solve their questions,
therefore, they might not directly express their doubts.
Because sometimes it’s the politicians from the town hall who give the infor-
mation, but they don’t have a technical background, so even if you have a
doubt you don’t share it because they also wouldn’t know - Member
On the other hand, the energy sector is complex and very easily gets technical. Both
the town hall representative and the developer agree that there is a pedagogical barrier,
both in the diﬀiculty to grasp the concepts by citizens but also in the lack of experience
by the experts to do it.
-[another diﬀiculty/barrier] is to explain it. Explain it to the neighbours and
making sure that they understand. This is a change for all. For me the first
one. Now I understand a bit, but the truth is that they are concepts that I
struggle understanding, because in most cases they are not palpable” - Town
hall representative
The actors of the energy sector like us, like any other company in the energy
sector, we are quite bad at talking to the citizen. We have never done social
engagement - Developer
However, not only there is a diﬀiculty to convey and comprehend the concepts, but
there is also a need for more communication and engagement with the members through-
out the development of the project. For instance, the last meeting was in October 2021
when the members applied to be part of the energy community. However, there has
been no more information about at which stage the project is for the past 3-4 months.
Additionally, there is a wish to be more involved and feel more like part of a community.
You want to see some benefits. Or something going on. Like creating a
WhatsApp group, a community, something to create a relationship. But it
has not happened - Member
On the other hand, the procedure to apply is considered to be very simple. Addition-
ally, there was a special meeting to guide the citizens through it. Moreover, there was
the possibility to do it both online or going to the town hall directly. Thus, eliminating
IT literacy barriers.
It was very simple [the procedure to join]. You fill in a form and that’s it.
This was super easy. And with the social network and internet, then the
town hall makes the procedure easy. I think it was quite easy [for everyone].
Because people who knew and were interested, then everyone joined, and
when we saw the final list [of members] they were there. I don’t think anyone
said: I’m lazy to do it. - Member
==PAGE:87==
6.4 Community acceptance
76
For the success of the EC, trust in the town hall is key. This is because, as found
within literature [113], pragmatism prevails and the members are happy to let ’someone’
that they trust to lead and decide. However, this also has a negative effect in the sense
that members are less involved both because they trust the town hall to do it properly,
but also because there is the feeling that there is no room to discuss. This raises questions
whether the community engagement is just. Walker et all. consider processes just when
”meetings are accessible, decision-makers recognize the legitimate contributions of local
citizens, and public input have some bearing on final decisions” [108].
You have the safety that the administration is behind the project, that they
can pay technicians and everything will be good, legally. [...] There was
not questioning [regarding the project itself] because it’s something very
innovative, it’s not normal, and people could see that. I think the advantage
of this project is at it is the town hall who develops it, then you trust it -
Member
I feel like it’s very monitored. The technicians and town hall are the ones
who make it and they explain how it will work and how it will be done. There
is no room to participate. - Member
On the other hand, the developer envisions the future of the community with the
member having a bigger role on the decisions which aligns with the expectations and
wishes of the interviewed member.
Our idea is that when the community is working, we would have a meeting
once a year. We would review the results that we have obtained: energetic
results, economic, environmental, and so on. And then we also have the idea
to use this meetings to think about the future. Which other things could
we do in the community? What do you think? What would you like to do?
What wouldn’t you like? and use these meetings to do a bit of brainstorming.
-Developer
Finally, once the energy community is connected to the grid and the members start
receiving some benefits, the role of an app to view the energy flows is considered essential
for the success and transparency of the EC. Milchram et al. find that transparency is
determined largely by the design of the interface [85]. Thus, interfaces which are not
comprehensible by households lead to less engagement from the members [85]. Moreover,
information sharing is not only an ethical issue regarding disclosure, but also a key
component in the mobilisation of local knowledge [12].
This could be of particular
interest during the follow up meetings after the EC is operational, as it would give
information to the members that could be used to propose ideas for the future of the
EC.
We think that it’s important that there is transparency with the members
about what is going on in the community, because at the end in community
==PAGE:88==
6.4 Community acceptance
77
projects everyone should be involved, and the data helps you being more
transparent - Developer
Having access to this I think it’s cool. At the end its more didactic, to learn,
and see the power that is generated, how much do you get. And be in the
project and see how it works. […] I think it has to be done, maybe at the
end you don’t get as many visits [to the app] as you expected. But it shows
transparency. - member
6.4.3
Recognition justice
Recognition justice is a matter of explicit recognition, it aims to acknowledge inequalities
and their fair accounting when devising energy infrastructures or policies [11]. Energy
communities are expected to alleviate energy poverty, however EC that successfully
achieve it are the exception due to a lack of financial stability, personnel and knowledge
[122].
However, this has not been the experience of the municipal EC in Catalonia.
Energy poverty households are recognized and targeted directly. Therefore, ensuring
that they receive the information and have support throughout the process.
We have a report from social services that gives information about the fam-
ilies with energy poverty. They are the ones with priority. We detected 5
families [within the 500 m allowed by legislation], of which 4 accepted the
invitation. In this case, we followed with all the process to apply to be part
of the EC”- Town hall representative
On the other hand, the the town hall does not target other groups of society. It
seems to be the agreement that the majority of the members are between 30 and 50
years who have a bit of an environmental awareness. There seems to be a lack of younger
members, possibly due to a lack of economic and housing stability as observed by the
interviewed non member. In contrast, the developer and this report’s author observed
that the members have a more advanced age, and are most likely retired. However, our
opinion could be biased based on what was observed during the meetings we attended.
Thus, the members who have time to attend the meetings are not representative of the
community’s population.
More like retired, I’m not sure if they would reach retirement yet, but in
general older. I’m not sure why, maybe for an economic reasons, some old
people are more tight [with money].[...] Maybe because older people are more
motivated that young one. But I don’t know, maybe they have more time
and they can be more involved, because they have the awareness that they
need to do something for the planet. Like people more hippy, who think that
now they have to do something good for their grand kids. Or if it’s because
they want to save [money], because they have pensions and not that much
money. I don’t know. - Developer
==PAGE:89==
6.4 Community acceptance
78
Additionally, there seems to be a gender bias regarding the participation in the EC.
The attendees of the meetings were predominantly male and as well as being more active
during those. The same issue is mentioned by Capaccioli et al. who found that men
participate more active in the energy community and get credited more [11]. However, a
single observation is not suﬀicient to draw any definitive conclusions, but it does suggest
that more research should be done.
In the EU directives, EC are expected to put the citizens at the center of the energy
transition. However, for this to become a reality their barriers must be addressed and
recognized. The four main barriers encountered in the municipal projects are: regulative
and bureaucratic, motivation of the citizens, financial and epistemic. First of all, the
project has found important barriers to legalize their installations. This has created
unrest among the members who expect the project to be connected before winter when
the electricity bill tends to be higher.
-[A barrier], is the legalization and procedures of the installations. For ex-
ample, the PV sites in some villages are installed since June last year (2021),
but they aren’t legalized yet (January 2022). You have to consider, that ev-
ery month that goes by, you lose money, you are not generating, it’s stupid
to have it like this - Developer
There is an important [barrier] that is regulation. I mean, in Spain [...] there
is not a developed regulation for EC. And this is not a barrier, because you
can do things, it’s true, but you are in a moment when we are waiting for
something to happen. And when you wait for something to happen, it also
makes this projects slower, because of course, until you know what you can
do, well, I don’t want to get involved. - Developer
Secondly, to put the citizens in the center of the energy transition assumes that the
citizens want to be put in the center of the energy transition. This implies that the
members have the time and energy to be active and willing to participate. Therefore,
simple set-ups where the members do not have too many concerns might be necessary
for EC to succeed.
At the end what you are saying is that people have to care, and this we still
have to see if it’s true. Because getting involved in an EC, it always assumes
that the citizen will take some time to think about energy. I think there is
a challenge to see how citizens adopt this model. - Developer
I think they have to be active, like the meetings are not something passive
that you join and that’s it. You have to be active. They [the town hall]
tell you about the meetings, but then you have to join.
And sometimes
these activities, they [citizens] are already stressed and lots of things, and
it’s diﬀicult - Member
Moreover, the energy sector requires substantial capital to get the project started
and that is likely to be out of reach for most citizens.
==PAGE:90==
6.4 Community acceptance
79
A challenge for communities, for me, it’s the financing. I mean that economic
model, that’s what I do, the pay back of the investment is there, but you
need the money first - Developer
Finally, the energy sector requires the actors who would like to promote an EC to be
able to understand and know how to navigate it. Not only technically, but also legally.
However, that is not the case for the majority of citizens or municipalities.
Where I give the most value, rather than the economic help from Diputació
(supra-municipal entity) is the technical counseling from the company as well
as the legal counseling to set it up [the EC]. A town hall alone, would not
be capable to create such a system - Town hall representative
And the thing with local administration, is that it’s missing resources. The
municipalities want to do it, but they don’t have enough knowledge, or time,
or resources.
[...]
We have found that the role of provincial, regional or
supra-municipal public administrations are very important, because this can
enable the municipalities to do this things. Because they give you technical
support, economic support - Developer
6.4.4
Summary
Overall, municipal ECs in Catalonia have social acceptance at the community level.
Trust on the developer of the EC is key for the success of community acceptance. In
this case, trust in municipal authorities was previous to the development of the project
given that local administrations are generally trusted. However, the definition of energy
community is still being contested and criticisms arise when the main beneficiary of the
EC is the municipality. Additionally, member’s of the EC pinpoint the need to have
more community engagement and more involvement in the decisions and future of the
EC. How adding a battery in an existing energy community will impact the perception
of procedural and distributional fairness will be further analysed in chapter 7.
it was already in our plans [to put solar panels]. So that the pilot project
was proposed was perfect of us. We were sure from the beginning, even if the
project would fail, even if there was no public acceptance, just for the savings
for the town hall, it was enough to pursue the project. If we didn’t manage
to give away the 20 kW for the members, we would have used it for other
things [..]. What they made us realize after is that if we explained to people,
if we found this very easy models: to be able to benefit, it would be hard to
encounter rejection [by the citizens]. Only by paying a fee, without having
to do anything there is a saving [for the members], it will take more time or
less, but it will arrive [the saving]. Without having to invest or worry about
the installation, this is a way to make it easy” – Town hall representative
==PAGE:91==
CHAPTER 7
Discussion
This chapter discusses the implications of the main findings regarding social acceptance
of energy communities with collective batteries. After, the main limitations and future
work is presented.
7.1
Interpretation of results
The objective of this Master Thesis is to analyze social acceptance of collective batteries
in energy communities in Catalonia. Social acceptance has three interdependent dimen-
sions: political, market and community acceptance. It was found that on the Spanish
level there is political acceptance. There is a clear political commitment to modify leg-
islation to allow batteries and storage to access markets and lower their bureaucratic
barriers. Additionally, the government has opened tenders for subsidies for installing
batteries in shared self-consumption installations as well as developed a budget to pro-
mote energy communities through the PRTR. Additionally, existing energy communities
with shared self-consumption are starting to show interest about the addition of batter-
ies to their system. The reasons for such interest are twofold: a context of public funding
of infrastructure as mentioned above, and an interest to self-consume more energy and
be more self-suﬀicient.
The results for the base case show that additionally to the political acceptance there is
market acceptance, given that the feasibility study yields a positive NPV. Additionally,
it is shown that investing in PV and batteries simultaneously is more optimal than
just investing in PV. Furthermore, batteries reduce the optimal PV capacity which is
particularly interesting for locations where roof space is limited. On the other hand,
the market acceptance is completely tied to battery investment costs. It was found that
from an investment cost of 522 €/kWh the NPV was negative for the base case, while
for the case with price uncertainty it was not optimal to invest in batteries when prices
are 600 €/kWh. As a consequence, declining costs of li-ion batteries will play a crucial
role regarding the adoption of the technology throughout the territory.
Moreover, high electricity prices are a good economic incentive to invest in batteries,
given that the benefits of self-consuming are increased. Consequently, in a context of
high gas prices, together with the Russian-Ukrainian conflict and the interest of the EU
to become independent from imports of Russian gas in the following years , there might
be an increase of market acceptance of batteries as they play an important role to add
flexibility to the system which is now provided by gas.
==PAGE:92==
7.1 Interpretation of results
81
On the other hand, to improve the business case of batteries in EC it is fundamental
that arbitrage is allowed, given that by allowing arbitrage the optimal installed battery
capacity is double and the NPV is 5,000 € higher. Moreover, granting access to balanc-
ing markets might improve the business case of collective batteries. However, legislation
should adapt to allow batteries in shared self-consumption schemes to charge from the
grid. This would not only benefit the economics of the EC and its members, but also
would allow DSOs or TSOs to use such assets to their benefit. However, even if tech-
nically possible, it is still not clear whether there is a viable business model and its
structure: whether the battery will be owned by the EC and operated by a community
manager or aggregator who receives an economic benefit for operating it, or the battery
is owned by the aggregator and the EC rents it.
Furthermore, ensuring a positive bill reduces the profitability of the investment by
capping the revenues the member’s can have by selling excess energy. Consequently, it
also restricts the optimal investment in PV capacity. On the one hand, it might promote
the addition of more members to the EC to reduce the excess energy that is sold to the
grid at price zero. However, the members would be increased just to allocate the extra
energy of the summer months. This results in having to share the energy with much
more people in winter, when the production is much lower. On the other hand, the
Spanish legislation allows an EC to sell all their excess energy at market price. However,
to do so the EC needs to register as an energy producer, thus, the bureaucratic process
becomes more complex. Consequently, even if theoretically possible, it might be that
certain ECs face technical and bureaucratic barriers to access this type of compensation.
Moreover, the energy that is sold at price zero belongs to the member’s retailer, which
is extremely profitable for the retailer who then can sell it to their consumers.
Regarding community acceptance, the research conducted throughout the Thesis
indicates that the energy communities analyzed do have community acceptance. It was
found that trust in the developer, in this case the municipality, is key to the success
of the project. Therefore, if the project were to be developed by a private investor the
perception of trust and how it is affected by whether the developer is a local stakeholder
should be analyzed. It is likely that in that case transparency, mobilization of local
knowledge and thoughtful community engagement will play a bigger role.
Nonetheless, none of the ECs used for the analysis had implemented a battery, as
a consequence, adding a battery might change the perceptions of justice. On the one
hand, it might be an opportunity to improve procedural justice if the decision to add a
battery is taken considering the preferences of the members. Thus, it opens the door to
use community engagement to decide the future of the EC, which could include adding
a battery or participating in balancing markets. However, using the battery to partic-
ipate in energy markets might affect the perception of justice and transparency of the
community. A community battery with multiple stakeholders who have different objec-
tives - maximising self-consumption for the members and benefits from participating in
the markets for the aggregator - might create tensions between them. Consequently,
information about how the collective battery is operated in this case is fundamental to
ensure transparency and proper community engagement.
Moreover, li-ion batteries raise questions of environmental concerns regarding their
==PAGE:93==
7.2 Limitations and future research
82
components and their extraction methods, as well as, their potential to be recycled. As
a consequence, there might be reticence regarding the addition of batteries to ECs by
members and non-members of the EC. An important consideration for decision makers
could be to include second life batteries in the project proposal.
Finally, a battery can improve or worsen the perception of distributional justice. On
the one hand, a battery can be used to share the energy more evenly between members.
For example, storing energy during the day and send it at night for the members who
are not home during solar production hours.
However, it can also create imbalance
between members. As shown in the results, when energy is shared based on the hourly
consumption of members (dynamic sharing), members with high demand and demand
during hours of solar production receive more benefits than others. As a consequence,
in a case with dynamic sharing rules, the distribution of benefits among members and
how members with smaller consumption or night consumption are to be compensated
should be discussed thoroughly.
7.2
Limitations and future research
This section introduces the limitations as well as future research regarding data, the
optimization model, the feasibility study and community acceptance.
First of all, the data for the electricity demand of the EC is hourly real demand
provided by colleagues, friends and family. Thus, it is not from a real EC. Therefore,
certain community patterns, such as, a cold wave in the village resulting in all members
increasing their consumption, are not observed. Furthermore, the data provided might
not be representative of a real energy community given that it’s provided by friends
and family. Consequently, data might be biased and might not represent the profiles of
members of an EC, given that the data provided is mostly from young ages and dwellings
with 2 occupants.
Additionally, it was assumed that all members of the energy community have the
same buying and selling prices. However, only 11 million out of the 29 million domestic
Spanish consumers perceive the PVPC [138]. Therefore, it is quite unlikely that within
an EC all members purchase the electricity at such price. Having differences in tariffs
between members has a direct impact on the individual economic savings of the mem-
bers. Consequently, it would be interesting to consider sets of consumers with different
electricity tariffs to understand how it would affect the benefits between members.
Secondly, the optimization model does not consider the self-discharge of the battery,
thus not taking into account energy losses in the battery or in the electricity connections.
Additionally, the model is purely economic and does not consider technical restrictions,
such as voltage constraints of the grid. Thus, whether the EC is allowed or not to send
energy to the grid due to grid restrictions is disregarded. Moreover, the model has an
objective to minimize costs. However, it would be interesting to consider a bi-objective
model that considers both minimization of costs and maximization of self-consumption.
This is specifically relevant for municipal EC where the municipality’s energy targets
==PAGE:94==
7.2 Limitations and future research
83
take a more important role than in private initiatives. Moreover, future research could
include a community battery that participates in balancing markets and assess how it
impacts the self-consumption and savings for the members and the EC.
Regarding sharing energy within the energy community, it is important to consider
that the dynamic sharing rule is calculated using real consumption. This means that
the model has perfect foresight when sending the energy. In a real context, the demand
of the members would have to be either forecast which would result in a less optimal
sharing of energy or expensive measuring devices would need to be installed in the
members’ residences. As a consequence, future research could analyze how uncertainty in
member’s demand affects the investment in the battery, self-consumption, self-suﬀiciency
and individual savings for the members.
For this study a socio-economic analysis has been considered as the EC is promoted by
a municipality. However, in future research different business models could be considered
regarding the battery: owned by a third party and rented by the member’s individually
or by the community as a whole, or owned by the EC and operated by an aggregator
or a community manager. The feasibility study is considerably optimistic as it does
not consider the degradation of the battery. Thus, it assumes that for the 20 years of
the lifetime of the battery, the same amount of energy can be stored. However, real
batteries degrade and lose capacity over the years due to different operation factors
such as temperature, cycles or SOC. On the other hand, the model is more restrictive
regarding the positive energy bill constraint. Only energy costs are considered, however
energy bills have both an energy and power term. Thus, if both the energy and power
term were considered the members and community would be allowed to sell more energy
to the grid.
Finally, the community acceptance analysis was conducted before the member’s were
connected to the EC and could start perceiving benefits. As a consequence, it would be
meaningful to move from a snapshot analysis to a process analysis to include how the
perception of community acceptance changes over time by conducting interviews and
participant observation at different moments of the development of the EC. This could
be extended to market and political acceptance and investigate further how price devel-
opments in the future change social acceptance and delve into the dynamic relationship
between all components of social acceptance. Additionally, interviews with EC that
have implemented batteries or are thinking to do so, could add relevant information to
the research.
==PAGE:95==
CHAPTER 8
Conclusions
The purpose of this Master Thesis is to analyze the social acceptance of batteries in
energy communities. It is concluded that they have social acceptance in terms of the
three components of social acceptance: political, market and community.
The willingness of the Spanish government to modify legislation to allow storage
to access electricity markets together with the context of public funding of storage in-
frastructure proves that there is political acceptance.
Regarding market acceptance,
collective batteries have been found to increase the savings, self-consumption and self-
suﬀiciency of ECs by discharging during the evening hours when consumption is higher.
Consequently, they reduce the exports to the grid. Additionally, investing in a system
with PV and batteries is more profitable than just investing in PV capacity. However,
battery investment prices have a significant impact on the attractiveness of the invest-
ment. When battery prices are increased to 522 €/kWh, the investment is not profitable.
Moreover, when the costs are reduced by 25%, the battery capacity increases between 40
and 200 %. Allowing multiple stacked use cases for batteries, such as arbitrage together
with self-consumption, provides a much stronger business case for collective batteries.
It was found that enabling arbitrage increases the NPV by 5,000 € and the battery
capacity by 71%.
When defining how the energy is distributed between the members of an energy
community, there will always be bigger winners. However, it was found that sending the
same energy to all the members leads to the fairer distribution of economic savings, with
most of the members perceiving between 200 and 300 €/year in savings. Nevertheless,
this strategy leads to high disparities regarding self-consumption and self-suﬀiciency
between members, where members with low consumption can cover almost all their
demand. In contrast, members with high consumption barely cover their demand with
self-generated energy. Moreover, this strategy leads to the lowest savings, self-suﬀiciency
and self-consumption when considering the whole energy community. On the other hand,
sharing rules based on hourly demand of members lead to the highest savings, self-
consumption and self-suﬀiciency percentages. However, this sharing rule results in high
disparities between members regarding their savings, with a difference of 1300 €/year
between the most extreme members. Members who have high demand and consumption
during solar production hours receive the highest benefits with this rule. Finally, sharing
rules have a direct impact on the battery investment. Sharing rules that do not consider
hourly demand result smaller optimal battery capacities.
The results show that municipal energy communities have community acceptance.
The initiative was evaluated as a positive contribution toward promoting renewable
==PAGE:96==
8 Conclusions
85
energies in a local context and alleviating energy poverty.
However, the conceptual
definition of energy community is still being contested, and concerns regarding the mu-
nicipality being the project’s main beneficiary were voiced. It was found that trust in
the developer is critical for the project’s success. However, it also results in stakehold-
ers being less involved in the project’s development. Consequently, more community
engagement was found to be necessary during the development of the project and once
the members of the EC have been selected.

==PAGE:111==
APPENDIX A
Spanish regulation
A.1
Storage
The PNIEC and ELP determine that energy storage is one of the elements to achieve
climate neutrality. Therefor, to cover the minimum needs of storage, it is expected to
increase the capacity from the 8.3 GW of today, to 20 gW in 2030 and 30 GW in 2050.
Additionally, for the year 2030 the documents expect a minimum of 400 MW of storage
behind the meter.
The PENIEC approves several measures to promote storage. Measure 1.13 about
renewable energy communities introduces sand-boxes project for EC. This projects can
include storage, to study possible viable business models. Additionally, it specifies that
legislators will work on developing a regulatory framework to ensure that communities
integrate storage. Measure 1.3 about electricity grid adaptation for the integration of
renewable energies, considers the role of storage in the integration of RES. Finally, Mea-
sure 1.4 about developing renewable self-consumption, considers that the development
of self-consumption includes storage in certain cases. The PNIEC also includes the need
to develop a National Storage Strategy.
A.1.1
Storage behind-the-meter
This section summarizes the regulation regarding self-consumption and storage associ-
ated to it.
In the RD 244/2019 regulates the administrative, technical, and economic condi-
tions of self consumption, allows the installation of storage systems together with self
consumption only if the right protections are available. Additionally, storage will be
installed as long as the the measuring device is shared with the generation, border point
or with an associated consumer (article 5.7). However, for the installations that the
Complementary Technical Instruction ITC-BT-40 about generation installations of low
voltage doesn’t apply, and until the regulation on industrial safety and quality defines
the technical requirements of storage’s safety elements, storage will be installed so it
shares the measuring device with the generation (fifth transitory disposition).
Regarding economic grants, the RD 477/2021 (29th of June) approves the conces-
sion of economic grants to promote different incentives regarding self-consumption and
storage, as well as the implementation of renewable thermal systems in the residen-
tial sector. The program of incentives 5, considers the inclusion of storage systems in
==PAGE:112==
A.1 Storage
101
self-consumption from renewable energies.
A.1.2
Storage in-front-of the meter
The RD 24/2013 about the electric sector, introduces the role of storage in the national
framework. Additionally, it makes the planning of assets of the transmission network
binding, so future storage facilities integrated into it must be included in the planning.
With a clear economic character and with the aim of stimulating the investment
in new renewable installations, the RD 23/2020 where measures of matter of energy
and others for the economic reactivation are approved, modifies the system of auctions,
introduces new business models, streamlines procedures for installations and establishes
a new system of guarantees to prevent speculation with renewable projects, introduces
the figure of the aggregator in the legal framework and incorporates into law 24/2014
the figure of energy communities.
In addition, RD 23/2020 defines the “owner of a storage facility” and recognizes
how other agents (producers, consumers or owners of electrical networks) can also use
storage facilities. It includes articles aimed at improving and simplifying the authoriza-
tion procedures for the construction, expansion, modification and operation of electrical
production, transmission and distribution facilities. It also provides a simplification of
administrative procedures for facilities that are considered RD projects.
RD 960/2020 (November 3) which regulates the Economic Regime of Renewable
Energy for Electricity Production Facilities, has the objective to promote renewable
energy sources (RES). This includes renewable facilities with energy storage. It envisages
the launch of tenders with competitive bidding procedures.
In accordance with the directives of the PNIEC, in February 2021 the Energy Storage
Strategy is published which includes 10 lines of action and 66 measures that address
issues such as the participation of storage in the energy system, circular economy, energy
communities, boost of renewable hydrogen, development of new business models (for
example, the second life of batteries), training of professionals and promoting RD. The
document analyzes the energy system in from an integrated and long-term perspective,
defining measures for the effective deployment of storage.
A.1.2.1
Network connection
Regarding the connection to the electrical grid, the RD 1183/2020 (December 29), of
access and connection to the electrical transmission and distribution gridsi, regulates the
access of the installations of storage that inject energy into the grid. Storage facilities
are treated as production plants (Article 6.3), always taking into account the technical
access criteria because these facilities at certain times behave as demand facilities.
Hybridization of new or existing generation plants with storage systems is allowed
(Article 6.8). Article 27 regulates the hybridization of facilities, both in facilities that
have connection and access permits granted and those that do not. When there is hy-
bridization with access and connection permissions already granted, the article states
==PAGE:113==
A.1 Storage
102
that hybrid installations can evacuate electricity using the same connection point and
access capacity already granted, as long as the permissions are updated and meet cer-
tain requirements. For hybrid installations without permits, applications are submitted
according to the general procedure but financial guarantees are reduced by 50% (Article
28).
The RD 1183/2020 does not apply to storage facilities when these components are
fully integrated into the transmission network or are in island mode (Article 3.2)
With the Circular 1/2021 (January 20), the National Comission for Markets and
Competence (CNMC) establishes the methodology and conditions of access and con-
nection to the network of transmission and distribution of electricity production, this
includes storage facilities in the terms provided for in article 6.3 of RD 1183/2020.
A.1.2.2
Tolls and charges
Regarding the payment of tolls and charges, CNMC Circular 3/2020, which establishes
the methodology for calculating transport and distribution tolls, exempts the payment of
tolls on batteries connected to the transmission and distribution network (Article 2.2.d),
adapting to Regulation (EU) 2019/943. This applies to both charging and discharging.
Electricity producers with hydro pumping technology are also exempt from paying tolls
and charges for injections into the transmission and distribution network and for energy
consumed. On the other hand, tolls and charges are paid for the use of the grid by
self-consumer regarding the energy consumed from the grid and for the self-consumed
energy in the case of nearby installations through the external grid (article 2.3).
The first additional provision establishes the creation of a working group with the aim
to analyze the need to improve price signals considering the impact on self-consumption,
energy storage, consumption aggregation and demand response. Therefore, the system
of tariffs applicable to storage is being reviewed to identify weaknesses and legislators
will work to prevent the activity from being subject to double taxation or duplication
of payment of tolls and charges.
A.1.2.3
Pumping storage
According to the 3.1 operating procedure, pumped storage is considered a conventional
generation and later a unit of consumption. To participate in the market it is necessary
to be registered as a buyer or seller of energy, therefore, in the mixed pumping stations,
there is a specific purchasing unit for pumping and another for sale that includes the
hydro turbine. Given that it is not possible to distinguish the production associated
with pumping from the natural contribution.
A.1.2.4
Market participation
Currently, only hydroelectric pumping and thermal storage associated with solar ther-
moelectric plants can participate in the market. Operating Procedure 3.1 states that
==PAGE:114==
A.1 Storage
103
storage facilities (not including pumping) not associated with generation or demand
facilities must have differentiated programming units for deliveries and power outlets.
However, there are improvements to allow participation of storage in electricity mar-
kets, such as the modification of operating procedures 3.3, 7.2 and 7.3 and the publication
of RD 1183/2020 (access and connection to distribution and transport networks) .
Resolution of December 10, 2020 of the CNMC adapts the operating procedures to
the specific conditions of the batteries. Therefore, storage can participate in the markets
of secondary and tertiary regulation, substitution reserves and technical restrictions.
This change includes:
• Storage technologies are made up of differentiated programming units for deliveries
and power outlets.
• A new specific operating procedure (PO 3.8) which includes, among others, the
qualification tests for participation in the balance sheet markets, for the program-
ming units of demand response and storage.
• Definition of the technical enabling processes for storage systems connected to the
grid and the specific criteria for carrying out the quality checks of the measurements
of the physical storage units.
A.1.3
Future regulation
This section summarizes the proposed and regulatory measures to encourage the devel-
opment of energy storage technologies.
A.1.3.1
Legal framework
Work is underway to define additional issues such as obligations and rights of storage
holders, and the role that DSOs and SOs will play, taking into account the Directive
(EU) 2019/994 on common rules for the internal market of electricity. Additionally, the
role and responsibilities of the figures associated with the ownership and operation of
the storage facilities will be established. In accordance with Directive (EU) 2019/944
on common rules for the internal electricity market, the services to be provided by the
owners of storage facilities should be defined. This includes the aggregator and energy
communities, as well as regulatory agents (TSO, DSO).
A.1.3.2
Tolls and charges
The tariffs applicable to storage are being reviewed to identify weaknesses. Efforts will
be made to prevent the activity from being subject to double taxation or double payment
of tolls and charges.
Regulatory provisions for the development of hybrid storage facilities need to be
developed and adapted. In this way, the applicable tolls and charges should be clarified
==PAGE:115==
A.1 Storage
104
in cases where the storage is combined with RES that have a specific remuneration
scheme.
A.1.3.3
Market participation
Legislators will work to adapt the different markets (daily, intraday, local markets and
complementary services) to the participation of storage facilities and to ensure fair and
effective competitiveness with existing agents, considering that the incentives for storage
systems are greater the greater the temporal or spatial granularity of the markets.
After the approval of the methodology for conducting coverage analysis at European
level (approved by ACER on 2 October 2020), Member States will have to implement it
to know the level of current security of supply of the electrical system. If the result is
that there is a possible risk to security of supply, the articulation of a capacity market
could be assessed and justified.
This will be suitable to allow storage participation.
There is currently a proposal for a capacity mechanism in Spain.

APPENDIX C
Interviews
C.1
Interview guides
Interview with a member
• Personal questions
– Could you tell me a bit about yourself?
– Do you have a job, and where do you work?
– What did you study?How long have you been living in the village? Is your
family living in the area?
• Views on EC and energy sector
– What was your motivation to join the EC? (climate; savings?)
– When was the first time you heard about what an EC is? From whom?/how?
– Could you tell me about your experience with the EC you belong to?
– Has your perception of the energy sector (and/or transition to renewables)
changed since you joined the EC?
– What is your opinion about the energy transition in general?
• Procedural justice
– When and how was information about the EC given? Through what channels?
How did you feel about it? Did it help you in deciding for/against the EC?
– What is the role of the company? Or the municipality?
– Could you tell me a bit more about the (public hearing?) meetings? Which
questions were asked during the meetings? Who were asking questions? Who
are answering?
– What would you have done differently?
– Which barriers did you meet to access information?
– When and how did you apply to join the EC?
– Is there anything you didn’t like or were bothered about? What and why?
– How is the contact with the rest of the members of the energy community?
What do they talk about?
==PAGE:119==
C.1 Interview guides
108
– How do you see the community evolving into the future?
– Do you think the EC will have any impact on the development of village and
local area, and how?
– Do you expect to be monitoring your consumption more actively now? Try
to save more energy / save more money? Do you find that you are ‘well-
equipped’/have the expertise to do so, or what might be done differently in
terms of communication?
– Is the information that you receive e.g. in public meetings accessible (for
‘non-experts’)?
• Distribution justice
– How did you feel when you heard about the possibility to could join the EC?
Do you think everyone felt the same? Why/why not?
– How would you decide who is allowed to join the EC?
– What is your opinion on how the energy is shared? Do you think it could be
done better?
– Do you think that the costs and benefits of the EC are distributed equally?
– What do you think about the siting of the solar panels? Is that opinion shared
in the village? (Using public space for the benefit of a few)
– Do you consider leaving? Why, why not? Do the rest of the members think
the same?
– What do you think is the village’s general feeling about the EC?
• Battery
– What do you know about batteries? How do you feel about them?
– Do you think it’s a good idea to add a battery to your EC?
– Where would you think the battery should be placed in the EC? (Do you
know the size of batteries? )
– Is the platform for the energy flows of the solar panels useful? Is there infor-
mation you would like to have that is not there?
Interview with a non-member
• Personal questions
– Could you tell me a bit about yourself?
– What do you work as?
– What did you study?
– How long have they been living in the village?
==PAGE:120==
C.1 Interview guides
109
• Views on EC and energy sector
– What is your opinion about the energy transition?
– When was the first time you heard about what an EC is?
– What was the motivation to NOT join the EC?
– What do you think about the role of local communities to also participate in
the fight against climate change? Is this something that you are concerned
with?
• Procedural justice
– How did you experience the forming of the EC community? How did you feel
about it? Do you think this feeling is shared in the village?
– If you joined the meetings where information was given￿could you tell me a
bit more about the meetings? What would you have done differently? Which
questions were asked? By whom?
– In which cases would you have considered joining the EC? Why?
– Which barriers did you find to access information?
– If you did apply but didn’t get in￿How did you feel about it? Why do you
think you were not allowed to join the EC?
– How would you decide who is allowed to join the EC?
• Distributional justice
– What do you think about the siting of the solar panels? Is that opinion shared
in the village? (Using public space for the benefit of a few)
– What do you think is the villages general feeling about the EC?
• Battery
– What do you know about batteries? How do you feel about them?
– How would you feel if a battery was to be added to the EC?
Interview with the municipality
• Personal questions
– Could you tell me a bit about yourself?
– What have you studied?
– What’s your career?
• Views on EC and energy sector
– When did you start working with EC? And why did you decide to work with
them?
==PAGE:121==
C.1 Interview guides
110
– How has the experience of developing EC been?
– Could you tell me what your role in the EC is?
– In your opinion what was the main reason to start the project?
• Procedural justice
– Could you tell me a bit about how the process of setting up an EC has been?
– Which steps do the participants need to join the EC? How was this procedure
decided?
– What are the typical milestones to set up an energy community? Which one
do you think is the most critical one?
– Are there formal regulations laying out the process of public engagement in
such planning processes?
– What is the municipality’s interest in an EC? (climate, money…)
– What are the main challenges?
– I have seen in the webpage of the EC that you tackle energy poverty, could
you tell me a bit about how you do that?
– Could you tell me about the meetings after the EC has been constituted?
– How do you see keeping the community engaged, looking into the future? /
How do you see the community evolving into the future?
– What are some of the main opportunities and challenges of EC from your
viewpoint?
• Distributional justice
– How is it decided who joins and who doesn’t the EC? How was the process
of deciding it? What have been the challenges?
– How do you think dynamic coeﬀicients will impact the EC?
– Could you tell me about how the project is funded?
– How do you share information about the success (or not) of your project with
other villages interested on doing it?
• Battery
– How do you think a battery could change the distribution of energy in the
EC?
– How would a battery impact the EC?
– What would be the main challenges to implement it?
Interview with one of the developers
• Personal questions
==PAGE:122==
C.1 Interview guides
111
– Could you tell me a bit about yourself?
– What have you studied?
– What’s your career?
• EC in general
– When did you start working with EC?
– Could you tell me about the model of EC you are developing? How was the
process of reaching this model of EC?
– How has the experience of developing EC been?
– Could you tell me what your role has been?
– What do you think are the main barriers when developing an EC?
– How was the cooperation with stackholders? What have been the differences
between municipalities?
• Distributional justice
– Could you tell me how the community decides how the energy is distributed?
– How is the project funded?
– How is it decided who can join the EC?
– How do you think the energy sharing will develop into the future? How will
it impact the EC? (dynamic coeﬀicients)
– How do you see EC developing in the future? Which barriers are there to
create more EC/reach out to a higher percentage of the population?
• Procedural
– Could you tell me a bit about how the process of setting up an EC has been?
– Could you tell me a bit about the community engagement process? (which
steps are there)? Do you remember how the meetings are structured? Which
information is given?
– Which steps do the participants need to join the EC? How was this procedure
decided?
– How do you communicate about the EC and battery=
– What are the typical milestones to set up an energy community? Which one
do you think is the most critical one?
– How big are the meetings? Are they as you had expected?
– Which barriers do you usually find when organizing these meetings? And
during the meetings?
– Who do they collaborate with to organize the meetings?

– Could you tell me a bit about the members of an EC or citizens who partici-

Si vols parlar de tu, que fas?
Jo tinc 38 anys ara, i vaig estudiar veterinària. M’he estat movent en moviments
ecologistes i una part d’aquests moviments és el tema de les renovables. Sempre he estat
interessat amb aquest moviment, però tinc desconeixença tècnica total. I bueno doncs
al final a l’acabar aquí al poble, i no viure en una casa pròpia, ens hem hagut de buscar
sistemes per poder tenir energies renovables sense haver de pagar unes plaques. I es
quan va sortir aquest projecte de la cooperativa.
I com te’n vas assabentar?
Aquí al ser un poble petit, de seguida te n’assabentes. Son pares de fills amb qui
vas a l’escola, i se’n va parlant. I a més aquest ajuntament fa molta difusió. I al final
a l’Instagram, xarxes socials, i una resvista que està per aquí que cada 6 mesos que
reparteixen, hi van sortint moltes coses.
Llavors tu creus que la gent se n’ha assabentat així, de boca orella o
xarxes socials?
Jo diria una mica entre la gent que mou les xarxes socials, i potser la meitat de poble,
i l’altra meitat son gent més gran i se n’ha assabentat per les resivtes. Sobretot perquè
se la llegeixen molt i també del boca orella.

Bueno es que ara amb el preu que hi ha, doncs segur que ajuda. I es un incentiu. Doncs
si, sobretot el primer era una mica la part ecológica.
Quina motivació creus que tenen la majoria de gent?
Clar, la gran majoria te l’econòmica. Si que la gent té la vessant econòmica que es

el mateix exemple es un aprofitament d’un be públic. Doncs com un bar que te una

estudi econòmic que fa que aquest cos, sigui molt inferior a l’estalvi que es pot generar.
es calcula mes o menys que una família 180 i 200 € anuals s’estalviarà a la factura una

a cada llar. Per això dic, es una comunitat una mica estranya. Estranya en el sentit de

aparells, o equips de submesura o l’aplicació que el que fa es compara a cada moment o

Esta apunt d’arribar un vehicle elèctric d’us compartit.
Estem fent el mateix pas, estem fent que una necessitat que ha de cobrir l’ajuntament,

la inversió, ja fa l’aposta i després de compartir. Qui vulgui que es sumi a la iniciativa.
Que agregi la seva necessitat elèctrica o de mobilitat, que l’agregi que s’aprofiti. Així
evitem, sobre dimensionar, un cotxe a cada casa, o plaques a cada casa. Però si que
podem compartir.
No se si vols dir alguna cosa extra
El final es tracta de compartir, no quedar-nos les coses per nosaltres mateixos. La
filosofia que hem tingut des de l’ajuntament es aquesta. Utilitzar els recursos, que sen
benefici el màxim de gent possible. Evidentment hi haurà reticències, a molts models,
i que segurament hi ha maneres mes sostenibles, cadascú pensarà diferent, però creiem
que havíem de començar, ni que fos una cosa de prova i mirar com funcionava. I a partir
d’aquí veurem, i cap a on hem de tirar, si la gent es vol auto-organitzar, i com que això
entenem que es difícil, entenem que a vegades s’ha de fer aquest primer pas. I aquí hem
volgut predicar amb l’exemple, i ser els primers de dir que nosaltres ens sumem amb això,
i a partir d’aquí, intentem que no sigui nomes en benefici de l’ajuntament, que al final
acaba repercutint a tots els veïns, però que si a mes a mes en podem fer us particular.
Això ens ha permès una mica això, doncs conèixer molta gent, conèixer experiències de
molts municipis, cadascun diferent, participar en moltes reunions, xarrades, i que al final
tot això ens acaba enriquint. I aportant valor a les coses que fem. Aquesta es la idea.
Aquí vaig deixar de gravar. M’estava explicant com es el poble, i com va sorgir
No hem començat encara perquè hi ha hagut un allau de persones que t’entren una
instancia a l’ajuntament. que penseu fer amb l’energia ? ja esta be de connectar a grans
empreses? No hi ha hagut això. Realment que tothom tenia, per tant, com deia, hi
ha molt teixit associatiu sobretot cultural i esportiu, però i fins i tot, amb temes de
medi-ambient, en el sentit més de natura, hi havia una entitat que es deia amics de
l’entorn, una meandres. Hi ha entitats de respecte del medi ambient, i sensibilitat cap
a l’entorn, a la muntanya, que al final estem entre la muntanya i el riu, i per tant el
respecte per l’entorn el tenim. Ara el que passa, es que una consciencia, un activisme,
mediambiental, no l’hem vist. O costa. No hem vist que hi hagi algú que s’hagi auto-
organitzat per reclamar, o per exigir, i entenc que això costa. Es passar a davant, es
anar a picar pedra, anar a picar portes, i tot això genera un desgast. Per tant aquí
quan va sortir la oportunitat de formar part del projecte, des del primer moment ens
va interessar, ja entrava dins dels nostres plans, ja era la nostra aposta d’instal·lar
energies sostenibles. Que ens plantegéssim un projecte pilot ens anava com a anell al
dit. Si teníem clar, que encara que fracasses, encara que no tingues acceptació popular,
tan sols, per l’estalvi que generava per l’ajuntament era suficient per tirar endavant el
projecte. Si no col·locàvem els 20 kw (als membres) els haguéssim utilitzat l’ajuntament
per fer altres coses. Les plaques posades a sobre l’edifici municipals, que el que generessin
pogués ser per autoconsum. el que després ens van fer veure, es que realment si ho
explicàvem a la gent, si trobàvem aquests formules tant senzilles, de poder-te beneficiar,
difícilment trobaríem rebuig. Simplement pagant una taxa, sense haver de fer res, amb
això tenim un estalvi a la factura, tardarà mes o menys, però arribarà, i aquest estalvi hi

Es el que dèiem, que pràcticament ens coneixem tots, m’és molt fàcil. Hi ha diferents

Molts ja em consta que si, que tot i així, compaginaran una cosa amb l’altre, no hi ha

Parlant així de perfils, us heu trobat algun perfil d’algú que sigui molt

No, escèptic no.
El que si que hem trobat també es que hi ha com els extrems.

Ara comentaves el tema del finançament, creus que el projecte seria pos-

Si, aviam, possible si. El que passa es que s’ha de calcular a molt llarg termini. Si
descomptes l’import, mes o menys una instal·lació de les plaques posem que surt a 1000
eur/kWp, 40 kw, doncs 40.000 eur/kWp el que seria la part física, després hi ha altres
despeses. La part de la instal·lació, clar realment si nomes necessites la meitat, amb
20.000 eur passaries, si que realment, sense l’ajuda de la diputació que ha aportat la
meitat, el que seria l’amortització es duplicaria, trigaries molts mes anys a amortitzar-ho.
Per un particular això es mira molt, però una administració no tant, en el sentit que
mentre s’amortitzi durant la vida útil de la instal·lació ja es suficient. No vas a buscar
un benefici. Però el que si que has de poder ser conscient, acabes utilitzant diner públic,
i clar el diner públic l’has de treballar amb mesura. I per tant, en aquest cas ha sigut
molt fàcil tirar-ho endavant, perquè hi ha hagut aquesta subvenció. També es veritat,
que encara que no fos aquest tipus, totes aquestes iniciatives mediambientals, posar
plaques, d’eficiència energètica. Acabes trobant finançament, perquè d’alguna manera
son les directives europees que estan cap aquí. Es veritat que les administracions el que
hem de fer doncs és buscar. Buscar els finançaments i portar-los endavant. Per tant,
sempre es podran fer aquestes instal·lacions,. Ara be, jo crec que on dono valor, de
l’aportació de la diputació, mes que en el tema econòmic, es en l’acompanyament tècnic
com de l’empresa com tècnic com jurídic, a l’hora d’implantar-ho. Un ajuntament sol,
seria incapaç d’idear aquest sistema: jo poso plaques, com ho faig, comparteixo amb
els veïns.... per capacitat tècnica, no saps a quines portes has d’anar a trucar després
de les companyies, no saps jurídicament... aquest treball que s’ha fet radere, es el que
entenc que és impagable, es l’aportació dels euros per les plaques si no hagués sigut la
diputació, s’haguessin pogut treure d’un altre finançament. O un préstec o finançament
aliè d’una altra administració, la Generalitat, Europa... vull dir que, l’ajut principal ha
estat l’altre. Per tant i entenc que, ha sigut com un win-win, es a dir la diputació li ha
servit per que 4 ajuntaments hem fet de conillets d’índies per poder anar a veure quan
anem a implantar el model amb quines dificultats reals ens trobem, i a mesura que les
hem anat trobant doncs les resolem, i aquest camí es el que ha fet que ens beneficiem
uns i altres. Nosaltres per ser dels primers de poder-ho tirar endavant, i la diputació
perquè ha aconseguit marcar unes pautes i ara tenir més planer per estendre-ho a la
resta de municipis. I així esta siguent.
